16/11/2022

UNDP- UZB -03 #00054196

Feasibility Study Report

INTRODUCTION, SCOPE and METHODOLOGY
Improving power and heat supply appears to be a key for Rural Health Clinics (RHC) being
operational during working hours. Indeed, rural health clinics need reliable and uninterrupted power
and heat supply to provide the standard medical services. This objective was defined by the Ministry
of Healthcare of Uzbekistan within the framework of the ‘Health-2’ World Bank (WB) Project which
is focused on helping poor families to improve their health by developing health care system,
providing family members broad and good access to health care services as almost the entire
population is living within two kilometers of a rural health clinic.

The key objective set up by both WB Project Health-2 and the Ministry of Healthcare is to make the
medical equipment supplied by the WB and other donors, operational during cut off of the power
supply. This is an issue of power reservation and security but not replacement of grid with Renewable
Energies (RE) technologies. Indeed, almost 90% of rural health clinics are connected to the grid and a
first activity of the project is to identify how many hours the medical equipment is out of operation
due to power failures. Another question is about energy efficiency: in order to limit the substitution to
grid failures, it seems necessary to have an energy management approach and check on possible
replacement of equipment intensively consuming power with more energy efficient equipment.

The second condition that may limit medical equipment operational includes lack of heating during
winter season. As there is also lack of natural gas (mainly low pressure on the grid) there is lack of
heating and hot water in the rural health clinic during winter season, therefore, the staff in rural health
clinic is unable to work. Solar panels made in Uzbekistan can provide some warm water when low gas
pressure or lack of gas supply takes place during a cold day, and this will allow radiators not to be
frozen and destroyed. It could also be possible to save the natural gas in spring and summer time by
providing hot water directly from solar panels. For heating and hot water a global approach including
energy efficiency can be proposed in order to ensure the medical staff to be working and medical
equipment to be operational.

The scope of the feasibility study is outlined in the Terms of Reference for the International
Consultant (Annex 1). The first mission took place between 9th and 20th of April 2007, the second
mission took place between 20th of May and 2nd of June 2007 and the final mission will be held during
21-28 of August 2007. They were carried out by Yves LAMBERT, an independent international
renewable energies and energy efficiency specialist. It consisted of 10 + 12 + 8 days spent in
Tashkent.

The first mission took place partly in the field for visiting the five rural health clinics selected for the
project. The consultant worked closely with the government counterparts, project management,
national consultants and direct beneficiaries (mainly the Ministry of Healthcare) (refer to Annex 2).

The national and international consultants analyzed all relevant sources of information such as the
project document, national consultants’ reports, and other data considered as useful (refer to Annex 3).

The consultants observed the field conditions and elaborated activities to be implemented within the
project to select the best solutions for each of the five rural health clinics.

The output of two missions is a draft Feasibility Study Report, to be discussed and completed during
last mission. This draft Feasibility Study Report is composed of 8 chapters corresponding to the terms
of reference and agreed by the different partners of this project.

During two missions held, the International Consultant was accompanied by Project Manager and
often by UNDP Renewable Energy Advisor.

EXECUTIVE SUMMARY
The solutions identified to improve power and energy efficiency and their reliability in the five
selected rural health clinics confirm that problems can be solved with different technical equipment
depending on each rural health clinic peculiarities and medical equipment already installed or to be
installed during next three years.

9

16/11/2022

UNDP- UZB -03 #00054196

Feasibility Study Report

For power supply, installation of batteries/accumulators and uninterruptible power supply (UPS)
seems the simplest way, with some experiment using photovoltaic (PV) systems. Considering the
demand side, some refrigerators can be replaced but the main action has to be undertaken concerning
the lighting: replacement of incandescent bulbs for low consumption energy efficient lamps.

For heating and hot water supply several solutions can be tested: new efficient gas boilers with
regulation, boilers with combined burners, electrical heaters, solar water heaters, etc. On the demand
side, the main identified solutions include insulation of rural health clinic buildings and pipes,
checking efficiency and in some cases replacing radiators, controlling and regulating the temperature
inside the buildings.

In order to choose between the various solutions, reliable information was required: power cuts
duration and frequency, lack of natural gas or frequency of low gas pressure duration periods and
frequency. Unfortunately, such information is not available and depends on sources. Same difficulty
about electricity and gas consumption: data collected from various sources are not coherent, and
expenses for electricity and gas consumption do not correspond to real consumption. Therefore, we
have to select some assumptions and choose a solution to be based on the expected consumption and
saving, as well as on the other criteria which have been classified by stakeholders.

These different solutions are detailed in Chapter 8 “Selection of optimal solutions in selected rural
health clinics”. For UMAROV and BAYMUKHAMEDOV rural health clinics, as they are both of
type 2, we have limited the list of improvements to be made in UMAROV rural health clinic in order
to have a basic solution to be compared with BAYMUKHAMEDOV rural health clinic after
implementation of the efficient and innovative solutions proposed. DJEYKHANABAD rural health
clinic is installed in the same building that the Hospital, so solutions have been proposed for both
parts, and this may serve as an example for improvements to be implemented in other hospitals.
Solutions for PAKHTAKOR and JUZIM-BOG rural health clinics present some differences, so it
would be possible to compare them after implementation and monitoring; due to their localization,
results of monitoring will be useful for further dissemination, particularly in remote areas and in the
rural health clinics that are not connected to the power grid.

10

16/11/2022

UNDP- UZB -03 #00054196

Feasibility Study Report

BACKGROUND
At present, large-scale reforms are being executed in the public health sector of Uzbekistan.
Establishment and development of the rural health clinics (primary health care) is the priority of the
national policy. The strategy for the primary health care reforming is focused on improvement of
quality and effectiveness of the medical service provided to the population, and is based on
integration of modern medical technologies, preventive health care, training of general practitioners,
strengthening of financing and management of primary health care.

To carry out the reform, in 1998-2004 the Government with assistance of the World Bank
implemented a pilot project (Health-1) in five provinces of the country: Fergana, Navoiy, Syrdarya,
Khorezm and Karakalpakstan. The objective of the project was to examine and test how the primary
health care system in rural areas can be reformed. The ‘Health-1’ project proposed and tested
restructuring of the multi-stage health care system to the two-stage, by that reducing the maintenance
costs of medical institutions. In addition, the project introduced a system where people get the first
medical aid from a certified physician rather than from a medical attendant’s assistant.

Positive results of the project persuaded the Ministry of Healthcare to develop a new ‘Health-2’
project. A grant from the Japanese Government funded the project preparation work. The Health-2
project aims to extend and deepen the scope of the primary health care reform across all regions of
Uzbekistan. In line with the Agreement on Development Financing signed between Uzbekistan and
International Development Association (IDA), Health-2 project was launched in 2004. This project
will provide 2,200 rural health clinics with up-to-date medical equipment. Other components of the
project will focus on making medical products more accessible, continuous medical education and
improving quality of medical services. The project will also distribute best practices and lessons learn
on financial and general management received from piloting during Health-1 project.

The Uzbek Government and international donor organizations have allocated substantial funds into
rural health care infrastructure. However, the majority of rural health clinics have faced certain
problems with power and heat supply during the recent years. The reasons are, in the first place,
interrupted heating and hot water supply caused by problems with unstable natural gas and power
supply to medical facilities (low gas pressure and frequent power failures), especially in winter
season. Moreover, some problems with reliable cold water supply occur in summer season. These and
other factors have made a negative impact on quality of medical services provided, medical
equipment operation and capability of emergency medical aid.

Development and implementation of all possible options of uninterrupted and reliable power and heat
supply to the rural health clinics, including integration of modern energy efficient and renewable
energy systems (e.g. use of solar energy for electricity generating and water heating needs) into
existing conventional energy infrastructure, provides the potential for more efficient and effective use
of the medical equipment and more comfort conditions for physicians operation and patients
treatment.

However, rural health clinics in Uzbekistan have some peculiarities. They are rather well equipped
with various medical equipment such as emergency aid, laboratory equipment for medical tests,
sterilizers, dental and physiotherapy equipment etc, which consume quite significant amount of
energy. The average electric power required for this equipment operation is varying in the range of 7-
13 kW, and average thermal power is in the range of 16-50 kW. Moreover, rural health clinics differ
in premises size and facility design as some are located in the buildings built specifically for medical
purposes, others in premises adapted for medical needs. They also range in scale (large regional
health clinics and small rural posts). Rural health clinics are located in various climatic zones such as
foothills, mountain areas, deserts, and valleys. They also differ in the water, power and heat supply
infrastructure; most of rural health clinics are connected to the grid but heating is provided by boilers
firing the natural gas coming from gas pipelines.

Within the framework of the UNDP’s “Feasibility Study on Improving of Power and Heat Supply
Reliability in Health Rural Clinics of Uzbekistan” Project, technical and economic assessment of

11

16/11/2022

UNDP- UZB -03 #00054196

Feasibility Study Report

options have to be implemented in order to equip the rural health clinics with Energy Efficient (EE)
and Renewable Energy (RE) systems, notably the plants that transfer solar energy to electric and
thermal power that can be integrated into existing conventional energy infrastructure to improve
reliability of power and heat supply has been implemented.

Developing the options for reliable power and heat supply has take care that rural health clinics are
equipped with a quite big number of medical equipment that requires substantial amount of electric
power. It is also necessary to have enough power for lighting, communication, and in many cases for
reliable cold water supply. Therefore, a combination of conventional and EE and RE systems has
been considered. Thus, photovoltaic systems may be used for off-grid power supply for lighting and
emergency equipment operation in case of power failure. Solar collectors can be used for autonomous
hot water supply in summer season as well as for pre-heating of water for space heating needs in
winter season to reduce natural gas consumption. Moreover, some local and foreign manufactures of
PV and solar water heating systems licensed and certified according to national standards are
available in Uzbekistan. These companies are able to meet the future demand in such equipment, and
provide full cycle services such as installation, commissioning, operation and maintenance, and after-
sale service. The options proposed have been adjusted to the relevant budget resources available in
rural health clinics and to the budget for medical equipment. They have to ensure efficient use and
saving of energy and fuel, to be coherent with budget available and to be replicable at a large scale.

Feasibility Study Report

FEASIBILITY STUDY

1

Size and type of the 5 clinics selected, location, territorial
and climatic conditions and peculiarities

In the terms of references for the National and International Consultants the first action was identified
as selection of rural health clinics to implement the technical solutions to improve power and heat
supply. However, referring to the letter from the Deputy Minister, Mr. A. KAMILOV to the UNDP
Resident Representative in Uzbekistan, Mr. Fikret AKCURA the Ministry of Healthcare of the
Republic of Uzbekistan has proposed five representative rural health clinics located in Tashkent and
Khorezm provinces and in the Republic of Karakalpakstan:

1)

Tashkent Province:

• Bostanlyk district, “Djeykhanabad” rural health clinic premises have been adjusted to

medical needs, and it provides 10,000 people with medical services;

• Tashkent District, “A. Umarov” rural health clinic is a standard medical facility (type 2), that

provides 4,728 persons with medical services;

• Tashkent District, “N. Baymukhamedov” rural health clinic is a standard medical facility

(type 2) that provides 5,139 persons with medical services.

2)

Khorezm Province:

• Urgench District, “Pakhtakor” rural health clinic premises have been reconstructed for
medical facility needs, and it provides 12,000 people with medical services. The building
was premises for rural health ambulatory before reconstruction.

•

3)

Republic of Karakalpakstan:

• Kegeli District, “Juzim-Bog” rural health clinic premises have been reconstructed for medical

facility needs (type 2) and it provides 4,851 persons with medical services.

1.2 Representativity of selected rural health clinics

Considering the characteristics of the selected five rural health clinics, they can be considered as
representative for rural health clinics in general in terms of power and heat supply. However, they are
not representative in terms of the different types of rural health clinics (there is no rural health clinics
of type 1 and 4, and only two rural health clinics are of type 2 which represents 50% of total rural
health clinics).

1.3 Medical rooms priority and engineering and construction
characteristics of five surveyed rural health clinics

The priority rooms can be classified as rooms used for technologies of medical-diagnostic processes
(except the dental room) as well as rural health clinic receptions and chief physician rooms. During
the survey, rural health clinic staff recommended that laboratories, room for patients' examination,
procedure unit and physiotherapeutic are classified as the rooms of the major importance.

The following rooms have not been classified as prioritized ones: sterilization room, storeroom, some
of rooms that are not directly involved in the technology of providing medical aid (for instance, chief
nurse room), dining room, kitchen, and other similar rooms.

Supply side analysis: Availability and reliability of utilities

2
and renewable energies
Considering the objective of the Feasibility Study Report as the “improving the reliability of power
and heat supply”, the study has to consider the both sides: supply and demand. Therefore, Chapter 2 is
focused on supply side and Chapter 3 is focused on demand side.

The supply side analysis has considered the availability of utilities (power, natural gas, cold and hot
water) in each rural health clinic selected, and then we tried to estimate the reliability of power and
gas supply. This is quite impossible today because there is metering only in some of RHCs and there
is no control of the reliability. GJSC “Uzbekenergo” recognizes that power cuts shorter than two days
could happen, but they cannot provide give us with data on frequency and duration of such power
cuts. NHC “Uzbekneftegas” does not recognize that there are problems with low pressure of the gas.
Therefore, information collected by the national consultants came from rural health clinics’ staff
memory and some written notes provided by the management of the RHCs, and we are wondering
about the reliability of these sources of information. Estimations and different options have been made
and selected for identification of technical solutions. These solutions are recommended for
implementation during the second phase (piloting of technical solutions proposed) of the project in the
five selected rural health clinics, and they must be confirmed by proper metering and monitoring
before further dissemination during the third phase.

In order to complete this chapter dedicated to the supply side, analysis of availability and potential of
Renewable Energy Sources (RES) has been made, and it shows that the most appropriate renewable
energy source for rural health clinics is solar energy that can be utilized through solar collectors and
photovoltaic panels. The main data on availability of power, natural gas, cold and hot water in the five
selected rural health clinics is presented in Table 2.1.

Reliability of power supply

Power supply system available in all surveyed rural health clinics is provided through transmission
lines of the District Power Supply System (DPSS). Straight questions to rural health clinic managers
about restrictions on power consumption were answered negatively; none of the surveyed rural health
clinics has problems with power consumption restrictions neither for installed equipment nor for
lighting.

In accordance with the data received from managers of rural health clinics, reliability of power supply
in other rural health clinics is rather low, particularly in winter time. Credible and official reasons for
this factor are not known, such data is available for power generating, transporting and marketing
organizations only. We can only make the following assumptions:

•

•
•

there is a general deficit of power in the region in winter time – from November till March
inclusive;
there are power cuts at transmission lines and substations;
frequent power cuts due to malfunction in transformers in connection to their long exploitation
time.

Unfortunately, no records are available to support the considerations about the length of power cuts,
its’ seasonality, frequency, etc. Therefore, information about reliability of power supply is rather
subjective.

Below (Table 2.2) are the answers to the asked questions about reliability of power supply systems,
received based on the polls of the rural health clinics’ staff, as well as information received in written
from the rural health clinic managers.

The problems with periodic power cuts exist in all of the surveyed rural health clinics. The most
unfavorable period lasts from November till March for Tashkent province, for Pakhtakor rural health
clinic this period continues from September till April, while for Juzim-Bog rural health clinic this
period lasts from November till May. Four out of five rural health clinics have no problems with
quality of the supplied power, or such problems have not been mentioned. The exception is
Baymukhamedov rural health clinic, which faces this type of problems.

In all rural health clinics power supply may be switched off during both day and night times.

The most lengthy occasional power supply shut-down period was observed in Umarov rural health
clinic – 7 days. In two other rural health clinics located in Tashkent province (Djeykhanabad and
Baymukhamedov) such shut-downs had durations of 24 and 48 hours respectively. In rural health
clinics located in Aral region such occasional shut-downs had not been observed. In Umarov rural
health clinic the maximal length of “once a month shut-down” is 48 hours. In two other rural health
clinics (Pakhtakor and Baymukhamedov) periods of such shut-downs were 24 and 36 hours
respectively. In Umarov rural health clinic the maximal duration of frequent shut-downs so-called “2-
3 times a week” was 24 hours. For the rest of the rural health clinics such frequent shut-downs had
duration of 6-12 hours.

The most unfavorable conditions during a month without power supply can be observed in Umarov
and Baymukhamedov rural health clinics, the periods reached 20 days and 14 days respectively
(taking into account complaints made by Baymukhamedov rural health clinic about power quality).
For the rest of the rural health clinics these periods averaged 10±1.5 days taking into account night
time blackouts and 2.5-5 days without night-time blackouts.

Another problem may be the reliability of the distribution of power: referring to the example of
Juzim-Bog rural health clinic and the photo of the failing power transformer, power cuts could happen
due to the bad quality of infrastructure.

Reliability and Potential of Renewable Energy Sources (RES)

The most important potential for renewable energy in Uzbekistan can be summarized as follows:

Solar heating of hot water: Development of concepts for more competitive local manufactured
solar panels for heating low temperature hot water based on adjustment of the European solar
heating technology to the Uzbek conditions, has been developed during last years and it is now
operational. The performance ratio has been improved significantly.

As a spin off effect of the large-scale mass production of local panels, the manufacturers may
develop concepts for small-scale solar heating units (tank, panels and supplementary heat) to be
competitive on the commercial market.

Photovoltaic: The use of solar energy for electricity production may be an interesting
opportunity for providing electricity services in remote districts and, thereby, give a new
perspective in utilization and development of remote regions. Solar radiation in Uzbekistan is in
the range of 1.5 to 2 kWh/m2 per day, therefore, considering the efficiency of photovoltaic panels
(12%) and the size of panels (100 Wp = 0.8 m2), 1 kWp (kiloWatt peak) may produce an average
of 1.5 to 2 kWh per day (less than 1 kWh in winter and 4 kWh or more in summer). Watt peak is
a specific unit for PV systems and corresponds to energy produced from photovoltaic system
with solar radiation of 1,000 W/m2.

Passive solar heating or solar architecture: There is a potential for using common sense and
practice in the building design in order to utilize the solar energy as a passive source for heating.
However there is an even larger risk that bad design of the buildings and unsuccessful design of
passive solar heating will result in a considerable cooling demand in summer, which will require
much more fuel energy (to produce electricity) than the saved heat demand. Such solution was
not been examined due to the fact that rural health clinics are still existing and rehabilitation will
be limited.

Wind energy: The use of wind energy for electricity production may, like the photovoltaic, be an
interesting opportunity for providing electricity services in remote districts and, thereby, give a
new perspective in the utilization and development of the remote regions of Uzbekistan. The
technology is fully developed on the international market for quite large production of electricity
that is not in the range of needs for rural health clinics.

Biomass: There is a very little potential for use of biomass in Uzbekistan, however, it is a fact
that some of scare resources are utilized on the country side and that the farm industry produces
some organic waste, e.g. straw, which can not always be utilized. Therefore, the perspective is to
create a sustainable use of biomass by improving the technology and efficiency for the current
utilization of biomass and for using the waste products from farm industry, e.g. by introducing
more efficient stoves and ovens at the market and by manufacturing biomass pellets. Considering
the actual development of biomass technologies in Uzbekistan, we cannot recommend this
renewable energy source.

Geothermal energy: There are geothermal resources in the underground, which today is used
only for medical purposes without re-injection, but not for heating. The most realistic option for
utilization of the source is direct heating of low temperature district heating water via a heat
exchanger and with re-injection of the water. It is of no interest for rural health clinic today but it
could be useful for large hospitals.

Hydropower: Large hydro power plants have been successfully installed in Uzbekistan and there
are a lot of hydro power resources especially in mountain areas. Concerning the five selected
rural health clinics only one can be equipped with micro-hydraulic equipment: water supply for
Djeykhanabad rural health clinic is provided through a pipe connected to a mountain source of
water (a spring) situated approximately 150 m above the rural health clinic; water is stored in a
tank installed near the rural health clinic and the District hospital. So, the resource exists and is
reliable but there is no producer of micro hydro power equipment in Uzbekistan and no guarantee
of maintenance.

25

UNDP- UZB -03 #00054196

Feasibility Study Report

Conclusions: Considering the potential of different renewable energies in Uzbekistan (refer to
various reports concerning this subject) and the level of energy needs in rural health clinics, it
seems that we have to focus on photovoltaic and thermal solar panels in order to produce
electricity and heat. However, solar radiation is not constant and is not reliable, especially in
winter, when there is a shortage of power from the grid. Therefore, it could not be a reliable
and/or an autonomous solution and it must be combined with energy management including
storage of electricity or heat (hot water).

Other solutions like wind, hydro power, and biomass could be used for improving power and heat
supply in healthcare sector but required investments will be very high for a limited energy
demand in rural health clinics. Such energies could be more useful in large hospitals than in rural
health clinics.

Picture 2.1 Power transformer supplying Juzim-Bog rural health clinic has been installed
on unreliable and dangerous basements

26

16/11/2022

Feasibility Study Report

Demand side analysis; type and characteristics of

National Consultant/Expert on PV systems in his report available in the Annex 6. He has detailed
power capacities and power consumption for therapeutic-diagnostic equipment, ancillary
equipment (refrigerator, disinfection, medical lighters, etc.) and lighting.

Table 3.1 Data synthesis for rural health clinics’ power consumption

1) Power consumption of the prioritized equipment and of lighting in the prioritized rooms is

highlighted with grey background;

2) * is calculations for power consumption including equipment to be procured for rural health

clinics under the Project “Health-2”;

3) Power capacity of the installed therapeutic-diagnostic equipment and support equipment (see row
1) in all of the rural health clinics is almost at the same level with difference of about 10% (taking
into account the future equipping of Umarov and Baymukhamedov rural health clinics under the
Project “Health-2”);

4) Difference in installed power for lighting (raw 4) is significant (almost three times) and linked

with:

a) different types and power capacities of lamps installed in rural health clinics,

b) difference in rural health clinic sizes, and areas to be lightened (see the Table 2.1);

5) According to the total designed power consumption (raw 5), four of the rural health clinics
(except Juzim-Bog clinic) are at the same level: with the equipment to be supplied to Umarov and
Baymukhamedov rural health clinics under the Health-2 Project, the difference will not exceed ±
10%;

6) Two times less power consumption of Juzim-Bog rural health clinic can be explained by absence
of power-consuming and support equipment, which exists (or will be supplied to) in other rural
health clinics;

7) Minimal proportion of power consumption for lighting (raw 8) is in Pakhtakor rural health clinic

- 35%, while maximal is in Djeykhanabad rural health clinic – 65%;

8) In other rural health clinics this indicator varies between 40-50%;

9) Share of power inputs for lighting (raw 12) in common prioritized power inputs (raw 8) is rather

big and varies between 59-72%;

10) Thus, it can be noted that the share of power inputs for lighting is large in all of the considered
rural health clinics and that in all rural health clinics the system of electric lighting is not optimal
and shall be optimized. Without optimization, construction of any type of the economic power
backup system becomes a problematic;

11) According to the data presented by rural health clinics, actual averaged daily power consumption

(raw 13) is 2-4 times less than designed value of power consumption (raw 5).

This situation can be explained by the following reasons:

a) data on averaged daily power consumption presented by rural health clinics covers spring-
summer time, when less power is consumed for lighting in comparison with winter time. At
the same time, it is obvious that our calculations were prepared for the “worst-case” period,
which is autumn-winter time. According to our estimations, this factor may result in
difference of 20-30% between the actual and designed values;

b) during calculations of power consumption the periods of blackout were not taken into account
that reduces actual power consumption by not more than 25% according to rural health

28

16/11/2022

Feasibility Study Report

clinics’ staff as well as ours estimates;

c) rural health clinics’ staff provided us with the overrated values on working hours of
equipment and lighting, in practice the actual time is significantly less than the data used in
rural health clinic calculations.

Unfortunately, rural health clinics didn’t present any documentary data to support values on actual
hours of service for equipment. It should be noted that unreliable and unclear data on actual hours of
service for equipment will significantly complicate further work on selecting optimal options for
power backup in rural health clinics.

for heating and hot water. Lack of meters or availability of non-reliable meters does not allow making
a good estimation of boilers efficiency and building insulation. We have seen some local actions such
as plastics on windows frame implemented to limit heat losses but this can be significantly improved.

No credible information is available concerning the actual consumption of natural gas for heating and
hot water supply, therefore, it is impossible to verify the validity of the applied standards: 125 W/m2
or 250 l/day. We can just try to evaluate the evolution of the consumption during last three years
based on the expenses.

Table 3.3 Main theoretical thermal characteristics of rural health clinics

20
14-16

In order to provide a temperature of 20°C during working hours and 14-16 during rest of time, we
have to know heating demand and heat losses. Such information can be given by a thermal diagnosis
but the missions of national consultants and the International Consultant arrived too late in spring
(non-heating season), and we have to wait for a thermal diagnosis to be implemented during next
heating season. As an example, heat consumption (125 W/m2) is incoherent with natural gas
consumption but even this data on gas consumption is not credible or reliable. Therefore, actually we
can just estimate the real consumption and heat losses on the basis of theoretical norms and standards
applied for rural health clinic buildings, which are quite different from the European standards. Next
figures are just given to illustrate where the main technical solutions are: boilers efficiency can be
increased and heat losses can be reduce with insulation of ceiling and walls, and introduction of
proper metering; and estimated investments have to be confirmed through the thermal diagnosis.

Q1 = General heat consumption

These figures are only theoretical and have not been confirmed by information gathered in the rural
health clinics. What 125 W/m2 means? Is it an average for the heating season, is the consumption
corresponding to the theoretical temperature (-13°C), etc.? Efficiency of boilers can be estimated as
70 or 80% maximum, and heat losses through ceiling may represent 25-30% instead of 18%. All these
figures have to be confirmed with a thermal diagnosis in order to estimate energy saving potential.
Unfortunately, such diagnosis has to be implemented in winter season only; therefore, we have to wait
for next heating season.

As an example of incoherence: with a consumption of approximately 3,000 nm3 of natural gas in the
type 2 rural health clinic BAYMUKHAMEDOV the average consumption is 130 kWh/m2 during the
total heating season; however, for the type 2 rural health clinics located in Fergana region the
consumption is 15,000 nm3 of natural gas that is 5 times higher. Where are the actual figures? What
are the actual consumption and real thermal losses in rural health clinics’ buildings? Another
approach based on the expenses seems to be more acceptable but the comparison between rural health
clinics is not coherent (refer to Table 3.5 in Section 3.3).

Comparison between the five rural health clinics and rural health clinics located in Fergana region
shows a large difference for both electricity or natural gas consumption and the relevant expenses. As
there is no real metering (meters exist for electricity but how we can explain that consumption in
Fergana region is 10 times higher than in Tashkent district?), consumption has been estimated but
expenses do not really represent the consumption and cost of electricity and natural gas consumed.
The first investment has to be contributed into installation of reliable and controlled meters in order to
know the actual consumption and motivate the stakeholders of rural health clinics to monitoring and
reducing their consumption of electricity and natural gas. During the second phase (preferably to be
held in winter), the thermal diagnosis will allow identifying the main heat losses and heat
consumption before installing new equipment and regulation.

Feasibility Study Report

Emergency equipment and medical priorities

4
Based on the list of medical equipment to be supplied to each rural health clinic within the WB Health
2 project, we have prepared an abstract of all those units, which needs electricity to be operational,
and we have also discussed with the person in charge of this project what is the priority to be
operational for each of the units of equipment. The results of the medical equipment prioritization are
given in Table 4.1.

The priority equipment can be divided into two groups:

1. Prioritized therapeutic-diagnostic equipment can be classified as equipment, which is directly

used during treatment, medical aiding, and diagnostics.

2. Prioritized ancillary equipment can be classified as equipment, which is used for disinfection
of premises, medical lighters, and refrigerators for storing vaccines, serums, medicaments,
etc.

Dental equipment, equipment for sterilization of instruments, solutions, etc. are not considered as
prioritized equipment due to its high power consumption, and possibility of creating a reserve of
sterile instruments when power supply is available and in the amounts sufficient for the periods when
power failure happens.

project)

20.25/22.75

Based on the above mentioned, we have considered an alternative variant that is less power-
consuming:

• only electrocardiograph and equipment for analysis should be considered as priority medical
and diagnostic equipment; some of the units have their own autonomous power supply
(batteries) and can be used even during one hour at power cuts;

• only refrigerator for drugs’ storage, medical lighters and equipment for disinfection of
premises should be considered as important auxiliary equipment; depending on quality and
efficiency refrigerators can store drugs at the acceptable temperature during 4 or 6 hours
during power cuts;

• only laboratory, inspection, procedures and physiotherapy rooms should be considered as

important rooms for lighting.

Therefore, total power needs for medical equipment to be provided by Health-2 project can be
estimated as 20 kWh/day. Power needs for the first priority medical equipment are 3.4 kWh/day.

It has to be mentioned that number of working hours for the equipment indicated in Table 4.1 have
been assessed by the Health-2 project experts. At the same time, the energy consumed will be in 1.5-2
times less: for the first priority medical equipment the power needs can be estimated as 2 kWh/day
plus electricity required for lighting. With the replacing fluorescent bulbs and fluorescent tubes with
low consumption energy efficient lamps, electricity needs for the priority lighting can be limited to 1
or 2 kWh/day even for winter period. Thus, for a power cut of 24 hours a storage of 3-4 kWh
seems reasonable with a maximum of 5 kWh for Djeykhanabad rural health clinic considering the
power needs of this clinic plus electricity required for lighting in hospital as this medical facility joints
them both.

Several solutions concerning selection of medical equipment may reduce the difficulty with
reservation of electricity due to the unreliability of power supply: preference has to be given to energy
efficient equipment, or to equipment with autonomous power sources (having their own batteries
and/or their voltage stabilizers). Such solution must be envisaged for further calls for tender medical
equipment to be delivered to rural health clinics.

It is also necessary to have enough power for communication, and in many cases for reliable cold
water supply (e.g. cold water pumping in Juzim-Bog rural health clinic).

As a synthesis of this analysis, the objective is not only the reliability of power supply but more
globally the reliability of medical service from the staff of rural health clinics. Covering needs for
emergency medical equipment and ancillaries during power cuts may partly solve the problem but it
must be considered with the working conditions. Is it possible to assume medical service in a building
which is not heated?

As the electricity needs are limited and linked with duration and frequency of power cuts; however,
heating, providing cold and hot water must also be solved.

Another condition, namely lack of heating during winter season may limit operation of the medical
equipment. As there is a lack of natural gas (mainly, low pressure of the gas supplied from the gas
networks), heating and hot water are not available in the rural health clinic during winter season,
thereby, staff of the rural health clinic is unable to work and serve the patients.

According to the opinions of Health-2 project engineers, the priority rooms in terms of the hot water
supply and heating (in priority order) are follows:

1. Pediatrics room;
2. Inoculations (vaccination) room;
3. Obstetrics and gynecology room;

36

16/11/2022

UNDP- UZB -03 #00054196

Feasibility Study Report

4. Blood examinations laboratory;
5. Medical treatment room;
6. Doctor consulting room.

As the heating, technical solutions must be implemented in order to get a minimum of temperature (15
0C) inside the rural health clinic premises even during coldest periods to avoid freezing of indoor
heating system (pipes and radiators).

37

16/11/2022

UNDP- UZB -03 #00054196

Feasibility Study Report

5

Identification of stakeholders’ role and responsibilities

5.1

Identification of key stakeholders’ role and responsibilities

Establishment and development of rural health clinics to provide primary health care is a priority of
the national health policy implemented by the Ministry of Healthcare. During the first phase (1998-
2004) the Government with assistance of the World Bank implemented a pilot project (Health-1) in
five provinces of the country: Fergana, Navoiy, Syrdarya, Khorezm and Karakalpakstan. Positive
results of the project persuaded the Ministry of Health to develop a new “Health-2” project. A grant
from the Japanese Government funded the project preparation work. The Health-2 project aims to
extend and deepen the scope of the primary health care reform across all regions of Uzbekistan

Therefore, the key stakeholders for this project are:

• The Ministry of Healthcare, its regional departments, and the staff of rural health clinics;
• Regional and local authorities/municipalities;
• Power, heat, and natural gas utilities: GJSC “Uzbekenergo”, and NHC “Uzkekneftegas”;
• Financial institutions and donors (national and international): mainly the World Bank, UNDP and

JICA;

• Private companies and local manufacturers (equipment and services): JSC “FOTON”, SPE

“ENCOM”, etc.

What are the role and responsibilities of each of them?

• The Ministry of Healthcare selects the five rural health clinics and Health-2 project director and
procurement manager were in charge of the call for tender for medical equipment to be installed
in the rural health clinics. Unfortunately, energy efficiency or autonomy of equipment have not
been mentioned and included as specific requirements in the specifications. The Ministry (rural
health clinic administration) is in charge of selecting, planning and installing this equipment
especially in the five selected rural health clinics. Staff in rural health clinic is established based
on a standard procedure set up at the national level;

• Healthcare provincial departments are in charge of district hospitals and complementary
activities implemented by rural health clinics. For instance, they decide about replacement of the
coal firing boiler existed in Djeykhanabad rural health clinic (the total medical facility includes
hospital and rural health clinic). They are assigned with monitoring of rural health clinics. An
annual budget for each rural health clinic is prepared based on calculations made using the
existing norms and standards set up at the national level. A financial ratio (budget per inhabitant)
is determined each year for the various regions (Table 5.1).

Table 5.1 Budget per region based on number of inhabitants served by rural health clinic

(Notice: RHCs in Tashkent region were financed together with Central District Hospitals. Since 2007

Investing capacities of the province administration and rural health clinics are limited and they
must consider other possibilities to attract new investments. Budget for every province (oblast) is
a subject to approval at the national level, and it is approved at the corresponding Regional
sessions of local authorities. After identification of key figures, regional health authorities work
out average budget expenses per capita and this is a basis for budget of individual rural health
clinic;

• Rural Health Clinic: Staff list is developed in accordance with the regulations set up at the
national level and it is a subject of approval and endorsement by the Regional Health authorities.
Rural health clinic managers make decision over the issues of staff hiring, setting up their terms
of reference and salaries. Rural health clinic’s staff makes decisions over annual budget
allocations with the concurrence of Regional health authorities and it is now in charge of the
budget management (Table 5.2): each year they have to plan this budget (salaries/wages, social
security, purchase and replacement of equipment and lighting devices, maintenance and operating
(M&O) costs of medical products, costs of power consumption, natural gas or fuel,
miscellaneous), every quarter they check the reality of expenses. Working hours for staff
members to be set up in accordance with the national regulations: from 5.5 hours for dentists to 7
hours for non-medical staff. However, rural health clinic and staff working hours are decided at
the local level and can vary (“floating schedule”) but at the same time should meet the
requirements stated in the Uzbekistan Labor Code (40 working hours a week). Rural health clinic
manager also decides the opening hours, heating needs, and day-to-day decisions.

reliability of power supply: power supply interruption is accepted up to 24 hours! They consider

criteria for selection of solutions, they would give their preference to technical solutions which
may allow operating medical equipment as long as possible with limited maintenance and
operational costs;

5.2 Development of possible instruments to disseminate this experience

all over the country

Considering the several solutions to be tested in five selected rural health clinics, monitoring and
evaluation is the first action to be implemented in order to identify what are the most appropriate
solutions, depending on various criteria (refer to Section 8.1), and how it could be possible to
disseminate them taking into consideration the limited budget availability for rural health clinics.

As we have already mentioned, today we are missing of a lot of information to be required to choose
and decide about the optimal technical solution to improve power and heal supply reliability in rural
health clinics, therefore the first instruments would be a better knowledge of the situation and
introduction of metering, including:

•

•

•

•

•

•

thermal diagnosis of rural health clinics’ buildings;

exact identification of power cuts (duration and frequency during at least the “winter”
season – from November to May) and natural gas supply failure (metering the gas
pressure) in five selected rural health clinics as well as better knowledge concerning the
power failures occurred in rural health clinics;

installation and monitoring of metering for consumption of electricity, natural gas and hot
and cold water in five selected rural health clinics in order to compare the different
solutions will be implemented;

identification of real needs of power: working hours (per day or week) of medical
equipment, instant maximum power capacity and size needed for refrigerators, etc;

integration of new criteria such as energy efficiency and autonomy for equipment
(boilers, regulation, lighting, medical instruments, refrigerators, etc) installed in rural
health clinic into the procurement procedure for future calls for tender;

identification of difference between types of rural health clinics, climatic regions, main
diseases occurred in the different provinces.

Feasibility Study Report

6 Options to improve reliability of power and heat supply
According to the various national consultants, several options could be considered for power supply,
electricity usage, heat supply, space heating, and hot and cold water supply. Therefore, before
selection of these different solutions various options considering the identified problems of reliability
and level of consumption are described below.

6.1 Power reliability and supply

Considering the reports of national consultants (refer to Annexes 5 and 6), the major part of
information about power supply and data concerning the power failures and power consumption are
unreliable, therefore, we have to estimate duration and frequency of power cuts on one side, and
power consumption during these power cuts on the other side, in order to identify the options:

• Blackouts may happen almost every day and could last for long periods (this fact has an
impact on technical solutions using electric power accumulating systems, as no reliable data
available on precise periods of absence of power in industrial electric networks). The most
unreliable period of power supply lasts from November till March. This is 5 months or 125
days = 1,500 working hours. Occasional (extraordinary) blackouts are 168 hours (7 days).
Provisionally the most frequently blackout periods can be assumed as 6 hours. However, we
may also assume that it can be longer. Provisionally, blackout period can be assumed as up to
two days. So, two options have been envisaged: 6 hours and 2 days;

• Electricity consumption and power needs. Based on the data received from rural health
clinics, power consumption varies from 4.2 to 15 kWh/day (Djeykhanabad, hospital plus rural
health clinic), and the main part of power is used for lighting. If we consider the needs for
emergency medical equipment and support equipment (e.g. refrigerator), power
consumption is limited to 6 kWh/day (refer to Chapter 3) that means 2-3 times more than it
was indicated in the information received from rural health clinics; therefore, 3-4 kWh/day
seems a reasonable average power consumption. According to the PV specialist’ report, there
are from 19 (Juzim-Bog) to 42 (Djeykhanabad and Baymukhamedov) lamps inside the rural
health clinics with an energy consumption for lighting varying from 6.2 to 19 kWh/day! For
emergency needs, number of lamps may be limited to less than 10 units, if they are low
consumption lamps consuming 8, 12 or maximum 20 W for 4 working hours/day. Power
consumption for lighting may be limited to 1,000 Wh/day (2,000 Wh/day for Djeykhanabad
rural health clinic plus hospital), refer to Table 6.4. In order to select between the various
options, we consider that emergency needs for power are roughly 3-5 kWh/day. So, two
options have been considered: 1) 5 kWh/day and a capacity up to 500 W that will be used
during the emergency periods (6 hours maximum); and 2) 15 kWh/day and a capacity up to 5
kW that will be used during the longer blackouts (2 days or more) and for all equipment
available in a rural health clinic.

Under such conditions the issue of integration the technical solutions proposed into the existing
internal electrical network available in the rural health clinic could be simplified.

Identification of different options

According to the previous hypothesis several solutions/options could be envisaged:

• Working as usual, if the duration of power cuts is limited to 3-4 hours per day, the risks are
limited. Some of medical equipment has their own batteries, so they are autonomous during
1-2 hours, and refrigerators for drugs’ storage can keep acceptable temperature during 4-6
hours. This option has not been developed in this report but it could be acceptable, if there the
real improvement of power supply to be implemented by the GJSC “Uzbekenergo” will take
place;

•

Independent source of power, such as diesel or petrol generator, with small-capacity UPS to

42

16/11/2022

UNDP- UZB -03 #00054196

Feasibility Study Report

be used during start of generator. After 6 hours of operation the UPS (as source of power)
needs to be charged. It can be used again as an independent source of power after 8-10 hours.
During this time recharging of the UPS can be possible using a diesel/petrol generator of the
adequate power. In order to ensure reliable power supply for a period up to 6 hours during
blackouts, an automatic power generator run by petrol or diesel will be automatically
switched on/off during blackouts. Generator capacity could be from 1 to 5-8 kW depending
on the options. The generator requires periodical maintenance as providing with fuel and
spare parts as well as a specific place for installation. Special fire and safety precautions
should be followed. Generator service lifetime depends on number of hours worked and can
be around 2,000-5,000 hours depending on the maintenance;

•

In order to ensure reliable power supply for a period up to 6 hours during blackouts,
accumulators plus UPS of 10 kVA can be used for temporary (emergency) power supply or
similar one. If the UPS serves more than one consumer, the issue of cable configuration shall
be sorted out and warning system shall be enabled to inform about UPS conditions (e.g. about
accumulator’s capacity), if consumers are located in different offices;

• For larger power supply, it could be covered by using UPS of 10 kVA х 5 or 6 units, or UPS
of up to 50 kVA capacity; however, the cost of such decision very far exceeds 100,000 USD
and we even can not consider such option for capacity up to 5 kW for long duration
blackouts;

• Another solution is usage of photovoltaic panels plus accumulators in the various ranges of
options, from 500 Wp (Watt peak) to 1 or 2 kWp. Investment cost is high but considering the
reliability and lifetime of such equipment it seems interesting to test this solution in 2 or 3
selected rural health clinics. Average costs for a 1 kWp system is around 10,000 USD.

As the last three solutions, it is also necessary to sort out an issue of cable configuration to enable
connection of particular importance equipment excluding possibilities of unforeseen connections in
order to protect power supply from short circuits and overloads.

• Commissioning works can be arranged;

6.2 Optimization of power consumption

Optimization of power consumption (refer to Annexes 5 and 6) is one of the key issues to reduce the
cost of any standby system of power supply in rural health clinics.

Medical equipment for rural health clinics has already been selected and the procurement procedure
has been launched, therefore, we have to consider the specifications detailed in Table 4.1, and it
seems difficult to replace them now with more energy efficient equipment. However, power
consumption of medical equipment represents less than 20% of the total power consumption, so, it is
more important to focus on the other equipment and especially on lighting, which represents more
than 50% of the actual total consumption in rural health clinics.

Simple analysis of power inputs of rural health clinic shows that in all surveyed clinics there are ways
and approaches for their reduction, including the most important electricity consuming equipment:
refrigerators and lighting.

There are two most simple and widespread approaches to reduce the power consumption:

•

•

approach #1 lies in reduction of equipment’s hours of service, reduction of general lighting
system and shift (at least partial) to local lighting system (table lamps and luminaries);
methods and ways for implementation of the first approach are probably the main prerogative
of rural health clinic’s staff and they can be covered in this report only in the form of the most
general recommendations;

approach #2 lies in replacement of existing equipment with energy efficient equipment with
less power inputs. Methods and ways for implementation of the second approach depend on
rural health clinic’s staff and equipment providers and are mainly related to availability or
lack of funds for procurement of new equipment.

To this end, we would only point out that all equipment (both medical and diagnostic, and auxiliary)
used in surveyed rural health clinics, have been received from the WB Health-1 or Health-2 projects,
therefore, replacement could not be an objective.

6.2.1 Situation and optimization of refrigerators in selected rural health clinics

It appears from Table 6.2 that electric power consumed by refrigerators is rather high, especially at
those rural health clinics, where three refrigerators are operating.

Share of electric power consumed by the refrigerators for drugs’ storage only varies from 40 to 65%
of power consumed by auxiliary equipment in various rural health clinics (even after rural health
clinics re-equipping with specialized refrigerators).

At the same time, there are both specialized (medical) and domestic refrigerators, which consume two
times less electric power than refrigerators listed in Table 6.2, including:

•

specialized (medical) refrigerators of KYOCERA designed for payload volume of 39 l, and at
the ambient temperature of 32 0C they consume 280 Wh/day (without production of ice) and
470 Wh/day with production of ice;

• domestic refrigerators, type ‘Saratov’-452KSh120 designed for payload volume of 100 l and

consuming 630 Wh/day.

Therefore, it is easy to reduce the electricity consumed for priority auxiliary equipment by 500-700
Wh/day using the above specialized refrigerator, and on that basis either to reduce the capacity of
standby power supply (and, therefore, its cost), or to include additionally some the second priority
equipment to the list of the first priority equipment, for example, a cabinet for sterilizing oven.

In order to use medical refrigerator during blackouts, an automatically controlled generator to switch
on/off power shall be needed.

6.2.2 Optimization and replacement of electric lighting system

Considering the average consumption for lighting in the five selected rural health clinics, which
varies from 30% (in Pakhtakor rural health clinic) to 65% (in Djeykhanabad clinic), replacement
with efficient electric lighting system is one of the most important issues for elaboration of
efficient standby power supply. The most radical one is to use so-called power-saving fluorescent
lamps (PSL).

These lamps are characterized with two features:

•

•

they possess all lighting technology characteristics of lamps normally applied for lighting
(for example, filament lamps or tube fluorescent lamps) but consume substantively less
(up to five times) electric power;
their nominal operating duration stated in specifications is 8,000-10,000 hours.

As a comparison, filament lamps (FL) have a stated nominal operating duration of 1,000 hours,
and tube fluorescent lamps (TFL) – 8,000-10,000 hours.

Naturally, application of these lamps should be primarily determined by reasons of economy (cost
benefit analysis), although their current cost in Uzbekistan is 10-40 times higher than the cost of
filament lamps and 2-8 times higher than the cost of tube fluorescent lamps. At Uzbek markets
power-saving fluorescent lamps (PSL) are sold by three groups of suppliers:

• Chinese power-saving fluorescent lamps are the cheapest (1.8-2.5 USD depending on the
capacity), and at the same time they are most unreliable. Their realistic average lifetime
does not exceed 2-30,000 hours. Moreover, in most cases, their starting controllers do not
comply with security requirements;

• Local power-saving fluorescent lamps (manufactured by the OJSC “FOTON”) cost 4.0-
4.5 USD, their average lifetime is 4.5-5,000 hours, and they fully comply with security
standards of IEC 928:1995, IDT;

• European power-saving fluorescent lamps (Philips, Osram, GE, and Siemens) available at
Uzbek market cost 8-9 USD, their average lifetime is 7-9,000 hours, and they fully
comply with security standards of IEC 928:1995, IDT.

To have a clear picture, cost of filament lamps at Uzbek market is 0.24-0.28 USD, and cost of
tube fluorescent lamps is 1.0-1.2 USD. The main characteristics of lamps for lighting are given in
Table 6.3.

Based on the data above, replacement of filament lamps and tube fluorescent lamps could become
economically sound and rational (refer to case study in Section 7.4). Even the cost of electricity in
Uzbekistan is 5 times lower than in Europe, replacement of filament lamps with power-saving
fluorescent lamps (locally manufactured) is economically viable after 3,000 hours (2 years) and
replacement of tube fluorescent lamps with power-saving fluorescent lamps is viable after 8,000 hours
(5 years).

An issue of lighting can also be solved through portable sources of light (lanterns or lamps with
accumulators). It is possible to use light emitting diode (LED) stationary or portable lamps, however,
the latter proposal is still under consideration as no data available about its’ reliability, though
lamps’ service life is almost 100,000 hours (according to the manufacturers) with 5 times less power
consumption in comparison with the luminescent energy saving lamps. According to our experience
the service life is about 3-5 years.

Recommendations on organization of lighting in the priority rooms of rural health clinics with the help
of the modern power-saving fluorescent lamps of 20 W (or less) are presented in Table 6.4.

Concluding the recommendations on power consumption in rural health clinics, it should be noted
that, even with power-saving fluorescent lamps, there are some reserves dealing with the optimization
of power inputs for lighting. Those reserves mainly lie in the planning of optimization of
working/lighting time for some rooms, reduction of capacity of applied power-saving fluorescent
lamps as well as wider use of natural lighting. Introduction of these facilities would allow reducing
power inputs for lighting for some 20-30%.

for the four of five rural health clinics, maximal daily deficiency of electricity is close to 5

reasons.

other reasons.

Based on that, we shall consider an alternative variant that is less power-consuming. This variant is
based on proposals made by the senior managers of Umarov and Baymukhamedov rural health
clinics. An essence of the proposals comprises of:

• only the first priority medical equipment (electrocardiograph and equipment for medical
analysis) should be considered as an important for emergency (consumption basis is
procurement for Health-2 project);

• only refrigerator for drugs’ storage, medical lighters and equipment for disinfection of

premises should be considered as an important auxiliary equipment;

• only laboratory, inspection, procedures and physiotherapy rooms should be considered as the

important rooms for lighting.

power supply (based on the monthly deficiency of electric power related to the power cuts) by
almost 1.7 times.

6.3 Heat supply

Four of the five selected rural health clinics are supplied with natural gas but during winter season
they face the problems of low pressure of the gas, so the boilers cannot properly operate. All gas
boilers are quite obsolete and they are not energy efficient.

In the fifth rural health clinic (Djeykhanabad clinic) the coal firing boiler was out of order and must
be replaced. It has been decided to replace with a new coal firing home made boiler (what about its
efficiency?), which will provide both hospital and rural health clinic with heating and hot water. As
the efficiency of a coal boiler is poor at low level of capacity, it seems preferable to install another
equipment providing hot water during non-heating season.

Business as usual option could appear to be a solution considering the answer of NHC
“Uzbekneftegas”, however, in fact in order to ensure the reliability of services provided to the
population by the rural health clinics, we must identify and select solutions for heating even when low
gas pressure is lower the working/security level for boilers’ operation.

Thus, possible solutions are described in Table 6.7.

Table 6.7 Heat supply solutions

Solution/Options

Thermal diagnosis

Installation of gas meters

Gas vessels

was too late to implement them during last season, but they must be
done in the five selected RHCs by the same time of installation of
meters, in order to have a base of comparison before installation of
improving equipment.
In order to identify the real consumption of gas in the selected RHCs
and to evaluate the main changes after installation of new solutions,
it is necessary to install reliable meters and to register the data every
week especially during heating season.

liquefied gas. However, there are no conditions to store liquefied gas

down to 50-60 mm in Umarov and Baynukhamedov RHCs.
This happens because the RHCs are located at the ends of gas supply
lines, and there is no possibility to “loop back the pipelines” – to
connect two different branches of gas pipeline to make a loop for
improving gas pressure.
As self-made natural gas firing boilers have been installed in the
RHCs, the Ministry of Healthcare has to replace them with more
efficient boilers. In order to avoid large investment a first option
could be just to replace natural gas burners with automatic control
devices. Price of one set varies from 280 to 320 USD.
Second option: replacement of the existing boilers. Boiler suppliers
are listed below in the Specification Table 1 refer to Annex 8.

boilers in Pakhtakor and Juzim-Bog RHCs.
This replacement has to be realized before next heating season in
Djeykhanabad RHC and hospital. In Djeykhanabad RHC electric
boilers could have been used as main source due to existing own
transformer stations. This solution has not been chosen by
stakeholders.
Electrical boilers (with the capacity of 25 kW manufactured in
Russia or in Uzbekistan), solar water heaters can be installed for
production and storage (as reserve unit) of hot water for the central
heating of RHCs during shortage or low pressure period.
In order to provide central heating with hot water and to avoid
freezing in the grid it could be possible to storage hot water at 60-65
0C for entering in the grid if the temperature goes down 10 or 5 0C
inside the buildings. A storage tank of 2 or 3,000 liters seems a
minimum to be installed with an automatic regulation.

Theoretical consumption of hot water in RHCs is 250 liters per day,

To improve reliability of RHC hot water supply, electric water

54

16/11/2022

UNDP- UZB -03 #00054196

Feasibility Study Report

accumulator tanks with the capacity of up to 50-120 liters can be
used. Heated water can be stored for a long period in well-isolated
tank. Installed capacity of electric heating elements of 1-2 kW
enables storage of water heated up to 65 0C within 4-8 hours.
They have the same advantages like electric ones. Heating is done
through popping of gas with automatics ensuring maintaining the
temperature at the same level. Disadvantage: gas boilers have to be
operational even in non heating season.
Solar water heaters with collector area of 1 m2 and tank capacity of
80 liters can be installed; or with collector area of 2 m2 and tank
capacity of 160 liters. Solar water heaters can be used seasonally or
whole-year. Seasonal solar water heaters are of very simple design
and low cost. Solar water heaters for whole-year’ use are of double-
loop circuit with tubular electric heating element in accumulator
tank which enables to maintain constant temperatures under low
solar radiation.
In several RHCs we have notice that staff is using gas cookers only
for getting boiling water, so it seems more efficient to provide RHC
with small liquefied gas boilers for this purpose. Estimated needs are
less than 3 liters per day, so life time of a small bottle of liquefied
gas could be 2 or 3 years.

boilers for boiling water

7

Financial and economic analysis

7.1 Methodology used for cost benefit analysis

“The objective of the cost-benefit analysis is:

To compare costs and benefits of using conventional fossil fuels, business as usual, energy
management and renewable energy sources for energy supply and demand and quantify potential
economic gains or losses on the state level, regional level and/or local level, which can be derived
through utilization of the Rational Use of Energy (RUE) and renewable energy sources, including
consideration of environmental impacts of the renewable and conventional types of energy”.

“The cost-benefit analysis on the state level includes socio-economic (or just economic) analysis, seen
from the point of view for the society as a whole. It is a normal convention in socio-economic
analysis that taxes, subsidies and possible incentives at national level are not included in the pricing of
fuels and technologies, as they can be seen as an internal redistribution of capital within the country
and will not influence on the overall socio-economic result. This convention is followed in these cost
benefit analysis; the analyses and assessments at national level include the assessment of reduction of
greenhouse gas emissions.” (Source: Renewable Energy for RHC. NREL USA 1998)

The cost-benefit analysis on local level (rural health clinics) includes financial analysis for the
investors. In financial analysis, tariffs for electricity purchase, fuel prices at consumer level, and taxes
are included in the pricing of purchase of fuels and energy (but not subsidies considering that tariffs in
Uzbekistan are lower than at the international market) and in the pricing of purchase of technologies.

The methodology, which has been used for carrying out the cost benefit analysis, can be briefly
summarized as follows:

1. Selection and definition of a number of case studies covering the major part of relevant
solutions selected for rural health clinics. Case studies for power supply using diesel
generator and solar PV system in the Uzbek context. Each case study includes business as
usual that is compared with the rational use of energy technologies and/or renewable energy
system;

2. Determination of requirements to financial viability (willingness to invest) for different

stakeholders;

3. Determination of non technology specific assumptions, including:

• Current fuel prices;
• Current tariffs for purchase and sale of electricity and purchase of natural gas;
• Assessment of fuel price development and tariff development.

4. Determination of technology specific assumptions, including:

• Energy balances;
• Greenhouse gas emissions;
•

Investments and operation and maintenance costs (O&M costs).

5. Discussion of financial incentives requirements in order to achieve the minimum set of

requirements;

6. Analysis of economical viability and cost-effectiveness (in relation to CO2 emission

reduction).

The cost benefit analysis is prepared for this project with comparisons of technologies for production
of power or heat on the basis of the case studies (sections 7.3 to 7.6).

56

16/11/2022

UNDP- UZB -03 #00054196

Feasibility Study Report

7.2 Energy resource tariffs evolution and forecast

Although prices for energy resources and electricity in Uzbekistan have increased (from 400 to
500%) during the five last years, prices for 1 kWh of natural gas is ten times lower than for 1 kWh of
electricity, however, actually the energy tariffs are from five to ten times lower than in European
countries (Table 7.1). Therefore, energy savings and renewable energies are more difficult to justify
from an economic point of view.

Figure 7.1 Economic fuel and electricity prices

Considering the scenarios prepared in 2004 (Source: UNDP Policy brief “Options for Continuing
Energy Reforms in Uzbekistan”, 2007), prices for natural gas remain at the low level, and on the
contrary prices for electricity are at the higher level. As there are 11 kWh in 1 nm3 of natural gas,
it appears that the current prices for electricity are approximately 10 times higher than prices for
natural gas. So, for the cost benefit analysis, results and conclusions may be very different, if we
have to compare new solutions with the substitution of electricity or natural gas.

7.3 Case study: power substitution with diesel generator, accumulator

or solar photovoltaic systems

To provide power in a rural health clinic during power cuts, several solutions have been selected:
diesel/petrol generator, accumulators, accumulators with uninterruptible power supply (UPS), and
photovoltaic (PV) systems with accumulators. In order to compare these solutions, we have
considered that they have to operate during approximately 1,000 hours per year (cumulative
duration of power cuts) providing 5,000 kWh/year (5 kWh consumed during 1 hour power cuts).

The technical and economic assumptions used in the cost benefit analysis are summarized in
Table 7.2.

solution is more economically competitive.

7.4 Case study: improving electric lighting

Considering that power substitution is very expensive, the first action to be undertaken is to
reduce power consumption in general and especially the power consumed for lighting, which
represents more than 50% of the total power needs in the rural health clinics. Therefore, we have
to compare cost of traditional lighting system with another one that is using the low consumption
lamps. This case study has the following characteristics: 16 lamps (minimum for emergency
service) as in the rural health clinics Umarov, Baymukhamedov, Pakhtakor or Juzim-Bog. Low
consumption lamps (PSL) can be provided by local manufacturers or imported from Europe with
difference in terms of prices and nominal/actual hours of service. Considering the implementation
of security requirements, a minimal operating voltage (170/160 V) and prices, comparison has
been made using the lamps locally manufactured (Table 7.3).

Thereby, without any consideration of reliability of power supply, it seems profitable to change
all lamps already installed in the rural health clinics with power saving ones. This avoids
replacement of lamps once a year, and save money and energy. This action requires more
expensive investments but payback period is only 2.4 years. Therefore, we propose replacement
of all lamps used in the five selected rural health clinics, as well as dissemination of such solution
through replacement of lamps in other rural health clinics when they are out of order. As
incandescent lamps have a lifetime of less than 1 year, they would be replaced within one year
quite everywhere.

7.5 Case study: heat demand saving potential using insulation and

automatic regulation

Three easy and low-cost energy saving measures could be widely implemented:

•
•
•

Insulation of attics: laying down a thick layer of lose mineral wool;
Improvement of window tightness by the mean of weather stripping;
Introduction of an automatic regulation for efficient temperature control in each rural
health clinic including metering and monitoring.

60

UNDP- UZB -03 #00054196

Feasibility Study Report

The only automatic regulation should be an imported technology. There is no doubt that a local
company could launch manufacturing of this component in some months, if a wide market is
developed for dissemination in every rural health clinic.

An average payback of these three measures in Europe is less than one year, due to the expensive
price for natural gas in Europe. In Uzbekistan, even the tariff for natural gas is lower than in
Europe; the Ministry of Healthcare could invest for improving the energy/heating consumption
with the running budget considered for 2-3 civil years. The rural health clinics’ managers should
pay on the basis of the records provided by reliable gas meters. However, today such decision is
difficult to implement because rural health clinics do not pay the real price for the natural gas
consumed: there is lack of meters or meters are not reliable, gas tariffs for 1 kWh is ten times
cheaper than for 1 kWh of electricity, and gas tariff is very far from the international prices.

As the figures concerning the gas consumption, efficiency of boilers, insulation of buildings, etc.
are not reliable, we must have elaborate and use the specific hypothesis concerning the rural
health clinic of type 2 (200 m2):

•

•

•

annual natural gas consumption for the rural health clinic of type 2 (200 m2) has been
estimated as 6,000 m3;

energy efficiency of locally made boilers is 60-70%;

energy saving potential through insulation of ceiling, walls, and windows is 20-30%.

Considering the rational use of thermal energy (heating), comparison of different solutions has

Even with a low tariff for natural gas, an automatic regulation presents a good payback period of
2.6 years, thus, it means that it must be implemented in the five selected rural health clinics and
proposed for dissemination in all other rural health clinics. The required investment is cheap and
can be included in the annual budget of a rural health clinic.

As the other investments and considering 2007 year tariffs for gas, the cost benefit analysis shows
that they could be done as an example for testing before further dissemination when tariffs for gas
would increase (in 2012). The first step could be replacement of burners only (instead of
replacement of boilers), with the required investment lies in the range of 280-320 USD, energy
saving potential is 15-20%, and payback period is reduced to 7 years even today.

Combining the three solutions presents a potential energy saving of 58% for an investment of
4,075 USD. With 2012 year tariff the energy saving could be 557 USD per year; and payback
period will be 7.3 years. Such investment is worth for implementing as a test in one of the
selected rural health clinics.

61

UNDP- UZB -03 #00054196

Feasibility Study Report

7.6 Case study: hot water production using electrical and solar water

heaters

Considering the potential of solar energy in Uzbekistan, use of solar collectors can save energy
for hot water production and for heating. However, the sun is more abundant in summer, the time
that is out of the heating season, and usually very few days can be without the sun during this
period. During non-heating season, hot water can be provided by autonomous solar water heaters
using simplified solar collectors installed on the roof or at the terrace of a rural health clinic with
the purpose to collect solar energy mainly during this period. Considering the needs for hot water
limited to 50-100 liters per day in the rural health clinic, a solar collector with the area of 1 or 1.5
m2 and with an accumulator tank of 80-120 liters will cover these needs. During heating season
hot water could be provided by a gas boiler.

Therefore, installation of solar water heaters has been analyzed in terms of two situations:

•

•

solar water heater is replaced with a gas water heater (it corresponds to the actual
situation taken place in five selected rural health clinics);
solar water heater is replaced with an electric water heater.

Technical assumptions are the following: Seasonal needs of 60 liters x 200 days x 55 0C = 540
Mcal = 628 kWh = 57.2 m3 of natural gas. Size of the solar water heater is 1.5 m2, and storage
tank capacity is 100 liters.

Comparison of different solutions for hot water supply has been made and is presented in Table
7.5.

Table 7.5 Comparison of solutions for hot water supply

The main potential for CO2 emission reduction comes from energy saving for heating, and next
