Conclusions on financial and economic analysis

From an economical point of view only one investment is attractive: lighting with power-saving
fluorescent lamps.

For electricity substitution, solutions are acceptable, if we consider a deficiency cost for kWh
during power cuts.

For other solutions, they could be attractive in 2012, if tariffs for natural gas and electricity
continue their increase in order to join the international prices (hypothesis of 400% based on the
situation took place during last five years).

Another consideration (and may be a financing source) is reduction of CO2 emissions; for one
rural health clinic it is only 17.2 tons of CO2 emission reduction but after dissemination in other
rural health clinics (in 3,000 RHC located over the country) it could be more than 50,000
tons of the CO2 emissions avoided.

63

UNDP- UZB -03 #00054196

Feasibility Study Report

8

Selection of optimal solutions and development of their
technical design

Previous chapter shows that financial and economic analysis limits the solutions to the
replacement of power-saving fluorescent lamps for lighting. The other solutions identified in
Chapter 6 present a payback period (break-even) longer than 10 years due to actual tariffs for gas
and electricity. Such approach is more positive, if we consider the lifetime of equipment (from 10
to 20 years) and tariffs expected by 2012.

However, the main problem to solve is reliability of power supply and heating, and the cost
benefit analysis does not take care on lack of medical services. Thereby, it appears necessary to
identify new criteria for selection of optimal solutions. After discussion with several stakeholders
we have prepare and tested a list of new criteria (refer to Section 8.1), and based on them selected
various solutions for the five rural health clinics (refer to Section 8.2). Details concerning the
technical designs of the chosen solutions are given in the national consultants reports (refer to
Annexes 5 to 8).

8.1 Criteria for selection of solutions

Technical, economic and financial aspects have been detailed in the previous Chapters 6 and 7
using a comparative analysis of the different options. After discussion with the main stakeholders
such as the Ministry of Healthcare, and the World Bank and JICA representatives, it seems that
we must have to consider other criteria than technical, economic or financial ones. Here is a list of
criteria and results of appreciation made by the stakeholders consulted (Table 8.1).

Considering the results of this consultation, it seems that quite all criteria selected are very
important or important. The first priority is given to the following: avoiding the medical risks,
reduction of economical damage, and reliability of equipment operation, which are very important
criteria. Demonstration of the technical solutions to be disseminated at a cheap price appears also
important. Then, the second series of criteria are lifetime of equipment (more than 10 years), its
easy operation and maintenance by local rural health clinic staff, and opportunity to reduce the
operational costs of rural health clinic.

In order to select the optimal solutions, we have placed them in Table 8.2 to identify what
solutions are corresponded to these criteria.

8.2 Optimal solutions and technical designs selected for five rural

health clinics

Combining the various options, characteristics of rural health clinics, and the above mentioned
criteria, a list of proposed solutions corresponding to five particular items, is given bellow. The
five items for improvement of power and heat supply are:

• Power supply;
• Electricity management and saving;
• Heat supply and space heating;
• Hot water supply;
• Cold water supply and storage.

For each rural health clinic, proposed solutions and technical design have been described in this
section but more details are available in the reports of national consultants (refer to Annex 5 to 8),
they can be used as a basis for future pilot project.

Alternative proposal:

For different reasons we have been asked to prepare an alternative proposal including a provision/
participation for the replacement of the coal firing boiler in Djeykhanabad which is out of order for
many months and cannot be repaired. Another reason is to reduce the amount of investment and to
guarantee the management and monitoring of the project.

In order to limit the amount of such investment an alternative proposal can be envisaged, reducing
capacity of some equipment (UPS in UMAROV, solar water heaters in Djeykhanabad, etc) and
focusing only on 4 RHCs considering that Pakhtakor is not representative of specific type and
location of RHC: type 3 and there are 2 other RHCs, situated in the suburbs of Urgench and not in a
rural area. A provision of 13,000 USD for replacement of the coal firing boiler of Djeykhanabad has
been added in order to answer to the demand of the Ministry of Healthcare/Regional Administration
(15 M UZ Sums+ 10% contingencies).

and monitoring the pilot projects to be implemented during two years, must be added. 12,500 USD
are dedicated to metering and thermal diagnosis (in the first proposal, reduced to 8,180 USD in the
alternative proposal), as a base for monitoring; so the cost for monitoring will be limited to
intellectual activities.

Indeed, after preparing technical requirements for equipment to be procured and announcing the call
for tender bids (3 months), signing the contracts and installing the equipment (6 months), it will be
necessary to monitor the pilot projects during one year to cover all seasons (heating and non-heating)
and to gain an experience from these demonstrations before recommending the solutions for
dissemination in other rural health clinics or hospitals.

75

UNDP- UZB -03 #00054196

Feasibility Study Report

CONCLUSIONS AND RECOMMENDATIONS
The main problem regarding this feasibility study is lack of official and reliable data that can be used
for identifying difficulties and possible improvements of power and heat supply. Every one agree with
the fact that there are failures with power supply from grid and natural gas supply from the gas
distribution networks (lack of gas or low pressure of the gas) but we could not get any officially
confirmed information concerning the power cuts (duration and frequency as there are no statistics
data for these problems), and lack of natural gas or frequency of low gas pressure periods. Even some
managers of the rural health clinics provided the project with some relevant figures, nobody knows
about the situation that will take place next 5 or 10 years. Therefore, we have to work with
hypothesis/assumptions only, and one of the first priority activities to be implemented during the
second phase of this project is to record (monitoring) duration and frequency of power cuts in the five
selected rural health clinics and possibly in some other representative areas.

Same problem with electricity and gas consumption: data collected from various sources is not
coherent, and expenses for electricity and natural gas consumption do not correspond to actual ones.
Thereby, we have no a sound basis to compare new solutions with the actual situation, e.g. what is the
real power consumption for lighting? How much the rural health clinics (depending on their geo-
climatic conditions) consume for heating? A thermal and energy diagnosis must be done in the rural
health clinics. Unfortunately, such diagnosis can be realized during heating season only, and it was
too late to make it during the first (feasibility study) phase started in April 2007. Depending on the
actual conditions of insulation of the rural health clinic buildings, and the energy efficiency of gas
boilers, solutions to improve heat supply are not of the same interest (from an economic/financial
point of view) to choose the assumptions and to select technical solutions based on the expected
consumption and saving, and on the other criteria.

However, as we have considered that the purpose of this feasibility study is improving the quality of
services provided to the population by rural health clinics, we proposed a list of criteria which have
been classified by the stakeholders (refer to Section 8.1.) in order to choose the solutions based not
only on the financial consideration, but also taken into consideration the reliability and lifetime (10
years or more) of the equipment, considerations for further dissemination, easiness for operation and
maintenance the proposed equipment by local staff of the rural health clinics, and opportunity to
reduce the operating costs (e.g. through using renewable energy sources). Potential for CO2 emission
reduction has also been identified in order to get carbon financing from the relevant international
funds.

Therefore, solutions identified and selected to improve power and energy efficiency and reliability in
the five selected rural health clinics prove that problems can be solved with use of different technical
equipment adjusted to the needs and peculiarities of each rural health clinic as well as to medical
equipment already installed or to be installed during next three years.

As for the power supply, installation of batteries/accumulators and uninterruptible power supply
(UPS) (proposed for two rural health clinics) with some piloting of application of photovoltaic (PV)
systems (proposed for two rural health clinics) seems the simplest way to test and demonstrate options
on improving power supply reliability in rural health clinics. Considering the demand side, some
refrigerators can be replaced with the energy efficient and energy saving ones, however, the main
action has to be focused on the lighting: replacement of incandescent bulbs with low consumption
energy efficient lamps has to be done everywhere.

As far as the heating and hot water supply, several solutions can be tested: new efficient gas boilers
with regulation, boilers with combined burners, electrical heaters, solar water heaters, etc. On the
demand side, the main identified solutions include insulation of rural health clinic’s buildings and
pipes, checking efficiency and possible replacement of some radiators, controlling and regulating the
temperature inside the buildings.

These different solutions have been detailed in Chapter 8 of the Feasibility Study Report and in the
national consultants’ reports (Annexes 5 to 8). As UMAROV and BAYMUKHAMEDOV rural health
clinics are the same type of buildings, we have limited the list of improvements to be made in
UMAROV rural health clinic in order to allow making a comparison with the BAYMUKHAMEDOV

76

UNDP- UZB -03 #00054196

Feasibility Study Report

rural health clinic after implementation of the technical solutions proposed. For rural health clinic
DJEYKHANABAD as a medical facility combined a rural health clinic and a hospital, solutions have
been elaborated for both parts. This can serve as an example for improvements can be implemented in
other hospitals. Solutions for PAKHTAKOR and JUZIM-BOG rural health clinics present some
differences in order to compare them before further dissemination will be recommended, and as
solution can be particularly implemented in remote areas and in rural health clinics that are not
connected to the power grid. Total amount of investments required for solutions for implementation in
five rural health clinics have been estimated to 132,935 USD.

An alternative proposal limited to four rural health clinics has been estimated to 99735 USD including
a provision/participation of 13,000 USD for replacement of the coal firing boiler in Djeykhanabad.
For this alternative the refurbishment of Pakhtakor rural health clinic has been deleted considering
that Juzim-Bog will represent a more interesting example for dissemination especially for non
connected areas.

In order to learn from this experience some funds for management and monitoring the projects to be
implemented during two years must be added, especially for one year monitoring the pilot projects to
gain the practical experience and reliable data and information before starting dissemination in other
rural health clinics or hospitals.

Annex 1. Terms of reference for International Consultant
Terms of Reference
for International Consultant/ Expert on Energy Efficiency and Renewable Energy Systems

Project Title:

Post Title:

A. Background/Overview:

Project of Government of Uzbekistan “Improving Power and
Heat Supply in Rural Health Clinics of Uzbekistan”
supported UNDP
International Consultant/Expert on Energy Efficiency and
Renewable Energy Systems
Development of technical, economic and financial aspects of
the feasibility study and carrying out of a comparative
analysis of all options to improve reliability of power and
heat supply for the pilot rural health clinics in the selected
regions
Tashkent, Uzbekistan
3 and half calendar months (15. 03.2007 – 31.06.2007-
approx.), 51 working days
SSA (full time for 51 w/d)

At present, large-scale reforms are being executed in the public health sector of Uzbekistan.
Establishment and development of the rural health clinics (RHC) (primary health care) is the priority
of the national policy. The strategy for the primary health care reforming is focused on improvement
of quality and effectiveness of the medical service provided to the population, and is based on
integration of modern medical technologies, preventive health care, training of general practitioners,
strengthening of financing and management of primary health care.

The Uzbek Government and international donor organizations have allocated substantial funds into
rural health care infrastructure. However, the majority of rural health clinics have faced certain
problems with power and heat supply during the recent years. The reasons are, in the first place,
interrupted heating and hot water supply caused by problems with unstable natural gas and power
supply to medical facilities (low gas pressure and frequent power failures), especially in winter
season. Moreover, some problems with reliable cold water supply occur in summer season. These and
other factors have made a negative impact on quality of medical services provided, medical
equipment operation and capability of emergency medical aid.

Development and implementation of all possible options of uninterrupted and reliable power and heat
supply to the rural health clinics, including integration of modern energy efficient and renewable
energy systems (e.g. use of solar energy for electricity generating and water heating needs) into
existing conventional energy infrastructure, provides the potential for more efficient and effective use
of the medical equipment and more comfort conditions for physicians operation and patients
treatment.

However, rural health clinics in Uzbekistan have some peculiarities. They are rather well equipped
with various medical equipment such as emergency aid, laboratory equipment for medical tests,
sterilizers, dental and physiotherapy equipment etc, which consume quite significant amount of
energy. The average electric power required for this equipment operation is varying in the range from
7 kW to 13 kW, and average thermal power is in the range of 16 kW to 50 kW. Moreover, rural health
clinics differ in premises size and facility design as some are located in the buildings built specifically
for medical purposes, others in premises adapted for medical needs. They also range in scale (large
regional health clinics and small rural posts). Rural health clinics are located in various climatic zones

78

UNDP- UZB -03 #00054196

Feasibility Study Report

such as mountain areas, deserts, and valleys. They also differ in the water, power and heat supply
infrastructure; most of RHCs are connected to the grid but heating is provided by boilers firing the
natural gas coming from gas pipelines.

Within the framework of the UNDP’s “Feasibility Study on Improving of Power and Heat Supply
Reliability in Health Rural Clinics of Uzbekistan” Project, technical and economic assessment of
options on equipping the RHCs with Energy Efficient and Renewable Energy systems, notably the
plants that transfer solar energy to electric and thermal power that can be integrated into existing
conventional energy infrastructure to improve reliability of power and heat supply will be
implemented.

When developing the options for reliable power and heat supply it will be taken in to consideration
that RHCs are equipped with a quite big number of medical equipment that requires substantial
amount of electric power. It is also necessary to have enough power for lighting, communication, and
in many cases for reliable cold water supply. Therefore, a combination of conventional and EE and
RE systems has to be considered. Thus, photovoltaic (PV) systems may be used for off-grid power
supply for lighting and emergency equipment operation in case of power failure. Solar collectors can
be used for autonomous hot water supply as well as for pre-heating of water for space heating needs
in winter season to reduce natural gas consumption. Moreover, some local and foreign manufactures
of PV and solar water heating systems licensed and certified according to national standards are
available in Uzbekistan. These companies are able to meet the future demand in such equipment, and
provide full cycle services such as installation, commissioning, operation and maintenance, and after-
sale service. The options to be proposed have to be adjusted to the relevant budget resources available
in rural health clinics. They have to ensure efficient use and saving of energy and fuel.

B. Overall Description of the Task:

The task of the International Consultant will be to ensure that essential steps in the process of
implementation of technical and economic parts of the Feasibility Study are undertaken, including

o carrying out of a comparative analysis (technical, financial and economic aspects) of
all options available for each type of rural clinics, and selection of the optimal power
and heat supply systems based on the comparative analysis;

o development of technical design for each optimal option selected to be implemented

within the future pilot project.

To this end, the International Consultant will be responsible for mobilizing and leading national
technical experts for planning and effectively undertaking the required surveys and preparatory field
work, establishing dialogue and a working relationship with stakeholders (funding partners, national
counterparts and local communities), and the timely delivery of the agreed documents.

The International Consultant will work under the Project Manager, in close collaboration with the
UNDP Renewable Energy Adviser and overall guidance of the UNDP head of Environment and
Energy Unit. The Consultant will supervise activities to be implemented by the Group of National
Consultants.

More specifically, the International Consultant’s tasks will include:

Task 1. Selection of pilot Rural Health Clinics based on their type and location.

The Consultant will study and analyze the following information to be collected and prepared by the
national consultants:

•

•

Information about the main types of rural health clinics (based on their size and type of
facilities) proposed on the basis of the preliminary studies made by the World Bank
“Health-2” project experts;

Information about the main types of rural health clinics with consideration of climatic and
territorial peculiarities of the regions (provinces) where the optimal solutions on
improving of reliability of power and heat supply will be implemented within the future
pilot project;

UNDP- UZB -03 #00054196

Feasibility Study Report

•

Information about availability of energy resources (power and natural gas) and energy
infrastructure (gas pipelines, grid and water supply networks) at the proposed rural health
clinics.

The expected output of this task will be

• pilot rural health clinics are selected based on their type, climate zone and territorial
location for which the options on improving of reliability of power and heat supply will
be developed.

Task 2. Analysis of energy demand required for RHCs selected

The Consultant will study and analyze the following information to be collected and prepared by the
national consultants:

• Number of problems and their root causes occurred with power and heat supply, e.g. how
often interruptions of power supply take place (how many hours, in what time of the day,
in what season of the year) as well as problems with the natural gas supply (interruptions
or gas pressure drops) for each type of the rural health clinics in each selected region;

•

•

Information about the total energy and heat consumption of the rural health clinics
selected including amount of energy consumed by medical equipment, lighting,
communication equipment, pumps for cold water supply etc.;

Information about hot water demand (daily and seasonal) and heating needs (area
required space heating).

The expected outputs of this task will be

•

•

analysis of the existing problems with power and heat supply is made for each type of
rural health clinics selected, and

energy demand required for improving of reliability of power and heat supply is
determined for each type of the selected rural health clinics.

Task 3. Design of options to improve reliability of power and heat supply in selected RHCs

The Consultant will study and analyze the relevant information collected and prepared by the national
consultants and implement the following activities:

• To assess local manufacturers’ potential to supply with the Energy Efficient and
Renewable Energy equipment required and/or availability of this equipment certified or
licensed according to the national standards at the local market;

• To assess needs and capacities of local companies to ensure after-sale service and to

provide the equipment proposed with the spare parts required;

• To develop financial and economic criteria to choose the systems that can ensure energy
and fuel saving and which are well suited for the budget resources available for O&M in
rural health clinics, and

• Based on the criteria developed to make relevant analysis of the Energy Efficient and

Renewable Energy systems selected.

The expected outputs of this task will be

• options are developed for each type of the selected rural health clinics to use of Energy
Efficient and Renewable Energy systems that can be integrated into existing energy
infrastructure to improve power and heat supply reliability;

•

•

list of local manufacturers and service companies is prepared;

capacities of technicians available in the rural health clinics has been assessed and
training needs are identified; and

•

analysis of the systems’ potential on energy and fuel saving is made, and

• budget to be required for the Energy Efficient and Renewable Energy systems operation

and maintenance is estimated.

Task 4. Financial and Economic Analysis

• To study current tariffs for electricity, hot water and space heating, and costs of energy

sources and fuel;

• To carry out financial assessment of total investments required for each option for

improving of power and heat supply reliability in the selected rural health clinics;

• To make cost estimates of O&M for Energy Efficient and Renewable Energy systems
proposed for each option on improving of power and heat supply reliability in the selected
rural health clinics;

• To conduct break-even and sensitivity analysis for the Energy Efficient and Renewable
Energy systems proposed for each option on improving of power and heat supply
reliability in the selected rural health clinics;

• To determine financial benefits due to fuel (natural gas) savings using the Energy

Efficient and Renewable Energy systems proposed;

• Based on the feasibility study results, to conduct comparative financial and economic
analysis of all options on improving of power and heat supply proposed for each type of
the selected rural health clinic.

financial assessment of total investments required for each option for improving of power

cost estimates of O&M for Energy Efficient and Renewable Energy systems proposed for
each option is made;

on improving of power and heat supply reliability in the selected rural health clinics is
made;

•

•

financial benefits due to fuel (natural gas) savings are determined;

comparative financial and economic analysis of all options is prepared.

and services), financial institutions (national and international), private companies

of EE and RE systems;

• To develop instruments to disseminate this experience all over the country.

instruments to disseminate this experience all over the country are developed.

C. Required Skills, Experience and Technical Capability:

The International Consultant must have a graduate academic qualification in energy, with
specialization in power and heat supply, and have knowledge of Energy Efficiency and Renewable
Energy systems. He/she must have knowledge of energy economics. He/she must have at least 10
years working experience in the field of energy and EE and RE systems/applications and relevant
working experience in developing countries.

The International Consultant has to have a sound practical experience in feasibility study development
in accordance with requirements and based on criteria applied by the international financial
institutions (World Bank). He/she must also have the relevant practical experience in development of
feasibility studies on use of EE and RE systems and implementation of the relevant international
projects, working experience in CIS countries is an asset. He/she must be a fluent English language
speaker and writer. Knowledge of Russian or Uzbek would be an advantage.

D. Reporting:

The International Consultant must ensure timely preparation and submission of a comprehensive
Feasibility Report comprising (i) a feasibility study for options on improving of power and heat supply
reliability in the selected rural health clinics based on integration of EE and RE systems into existing
conventional power and heat supply systems available; (ii) comparative analysis of all options
proposed for improving of power and heat supply reliability in rural health clinics selected; (iii)
technical designs for optimal solutions that will serve as a basis for future pilot project. All reports
must be submitted in English to the UNDP CO in Uzbekistan and made in accordance with UNDP’s
requirements to reporting.

REQUIRED SKILLS, EXPERIENCE AND TECHNICAL CAPABILITY

o At least ten (10) years of working experience as a specialist in the field of energy, and EE

and RE systems/applications;

o Academic qualification in energy, with specialization in power and heat supply. He/she must

have knowledge of energy economics;

o Practical experience in feasibility study development in accordance with requirements and

based on criteria applied by the international financial institutions (World Bank);

o Relevant practical experience in development of feasibility studies on use of EE and RE
systems and implementation of the relevant international projects, working experience in
developing countries and CIS countries is an asset;

o Good ability in partnering and networking;
o Proficiency in English, excellent drafting skills; preferably knowledge of written and spoken

Russian and/or local language;

o Excellent interpersonal and cross-cultural communication skills;
o Previous experience of working in Uzbekistan.

1.2. GENERAL, AND ENGINEERING AND CONSTRUCTION DESCRIPTION OF RHCs

1.2.1. Rooms Priority.

Priority Rooms can be classified as rooms used in technologies of medical-diagnostic processes
(except dental room), as well as RHC reception and chief physician rooms. During the survey
RHC staff recommended the laboratories, room for patients' examination, procedure unit and
physiotherapeutic room are to be classified as the rooms of major importance.

The following rooms are nor classified as prioritized: sterilization room, storeroom, some of the
rooms not directly involved in the technology of providing medical aid (for instance: head nurse
room), dining room, kitchen, and other similar room.

1.2.2. All engineering-construction characteristics of the five surveyed RHCs are summarized in
the Table 2.1 below.

1.3. EQUIPMENT AND LIGHTING POWER CONSUMPTION.

1.3.1. The data on design capacity and design values on power consumption for all and priority
equipment of the surveyed five RHCs are set out in Tables 3.1 and 3.2. The equipment currently
being used in the surveyed RHCs as well as the equipment to be procured for the individual RHCs
under the National Program Health-2 are also presented in the same tables.

1.3.2. Priority equipment can be divided into two groups:

1. Prioritized therapeutic-diagnostic equipment can be classified as equipment which is directly used
during treatment, medical aiding, and diagnostics.

2. Prioritized ancillary equipment can be classified as equipment which is used for disinfection of
premises, medical lighters, and refrigerators for storing vaccines, serums, medicaments, etc.

3. Dental equipment, equipment for sterilization of instruments, solutions, etc., are not considered as
Prioritized equipment due to its high power consumption, and possibility of creating a reserve of
sterile instruments during availability of power supply for the times when failure of power supply
happens.

4. Design capacity of the equipment was determined based on either the equipment passport or
equipment label. Hours of service identified based on the data presented by the management of the
RHCs.

1.3.3. Table 3.3 presents data on design capacity and design values of power consumption for lighting
all RHC premises and the prioritized rooms, where all the installed equipment shall be operational
during any power cuts of main sources of power supply.

All of the surveyed RHCs have lobbies and corridors of rather large size and without almost any
natural lighting. Therefore minimal lighting of these premises is also obligatory at least by reason of
movement safety. Based on this we need to take into account lighting of lobbies, corridors and stairs
while preparing calculations for design of power consumption.

Existing system of lighting of these premises under using reserve power supply is obviously
redundant, and therefore inappropriate. Thus, for instance, there are 10 incandescent electric lamps in
the lobby of A.Umarov RHC, while 19 incandescent electric lamps and 8 luminescent lamps in the
lobby of N. Baymuhamedov RHC.

We also need to note that in winter time when daylight hours are minimal, the action period of the
lamps should be not less than 4 hours a day.

1.3.4. From viewpoint of possible use of PV-systems for designing a system of reserve power supply,
the most important aspect is power consumption in autumn-winter period, due to the following
aspects of this period:

a/ high likelihood of power cuts due to its shortage and more higher likelihood of breakages (see
Chapter 4 of the current Report for detailed information);

b/ higher level of energy consumption due to higher common sickness rate (and consequently,
increased number of visits to RHC) and higher level of power consumption of lighting;

c/ the lowest efficiency of PV-systems, due to decreased solar illumination and increased number of
overcast days (see Chapter 5 of the present Report.)

Based on the abovementioned, maximal values (named by RHC staff) of working periods for
equipment and lighting were used in calculations of power consumption.

1.3.6. Summary Data On RHCs Power Consumption:

1.3.6.1. The power capacity of the installed therapeutic – diagnostic equipment and support
equipment (see row 1) in all of the RHCs is almost at the same level with difference of about 10%
(taking into account future equipping of A. Umarov and N. Baymuhannedov RHCs under the
Program Health-2).

1.3.6.2. The difference in installed power for lighting (see the raw 4) is significant (almost three
times) and linked with:

a/ different types and powers of lamps, that are installed in RHCs.,

b/ difference in RHCs sizes and the areas to be lightened (see the Table 2.1.).

1.3.6.3. According to the total designed power consumption (see the raw 5) four of the RHCs (except
Jusi Bog) are at the same level: with the equipment to be supplied to A. Umarov and N.
Baymuhannedov RHCs under the Program Health-2, the difference will not exceed ± 10%.

Two times less power consumption of Juzim Bog RHC can be explained by the absence of power-
consuming and support equipment, which exists (or will be supplied to) in other RHCs.

1.3.6.4. Minimal proportion of power consumption for lighting (see the raw 8) is in Pakhtakor RHC -
35%, while maximal is in Jahonobod RHC – 65%.

In other RHCs this indicator varies between 40% - 50%.

Share of power inputs for lighting (see the raw 12) in common prioritized power inputs (see the raw
8) is rather big and varies between 59%-72%.

Thus, it can be noted, that the share of power inputs for lighting is large in all of the considered RHC,
and that in all of the RHCs the system of electric lighting is not optimal and shall be optimized.
Without the optimization construction of any type of the economical power backup system becomes
problematic.

1.3.6.5. According to the data presented by the RHCs actual averaged daily power consumption (see
the raw 13) is 2-4 times less than design value of power consumption (see the raw 5) в 2 – 4 раза.

This situation can be explained by the following reasons:

a/ averaged daily power consumption data presented by RHCs covers spring-summer time, when less
power is consumed for lighting compared with winter time. At the same time it is obvious, that our
calculations were prepared for the “worst-case” period which is autumn-winter time. According to our
estimations this factor may result in difference of 20-30% between actual and design values;

b/ during calculations of power consumption the periods of blackout were not taken into account,
which reduces actual power consumption by not more than 25% according to RHCs staff as well as
our estimates.

c/ RHCs staff submitted overrated values on periods of work of equipment and lighting – in practice
actual time significantly less than the data used in RHC calculations.

Unfortunately, the RHCs didn’t present any documentary data to support values on actual hours of
service of equipment.

It should be noted, that unreliable and unclear data on actual hours of service of the equipment will
significantly complicate further work on selecting optimal options for power backup of RHCs.

1.4. CHARACTERISTICS OF RHC POWER SUPPLY SYSTEM AND ITS’ RELIABILITY

1.4.1. Power supply system of all of the surveyed RHCs is provided through transmission lines of
District Power Supply System (DPSS). Straight questions to RHC managers on restrictions on power
consumption were answered negatively - non of the surveyed RHC has problems with power
consumption restrictions for installed equipment neither for lighting.

1.4.2. In accordance with the data received from managers of RHCs, reliability of power supply in
other RHCs is rather low, particularly in winter time. Credible and official reasons for this factor is
not known – this data is available only for power generating, transporting and marketing
organizations. We may only make the following assumptions:

104

UNDP- UZB -03 #00054196

Feasibility Study Report

- general deficit of power in the region in winter time – from November till March inclusive (as it was
stated above in section 3.4.),

- breakages at transmission lines and substations.

1.4.3. Unfortunately, no records available to support the data about the length of power cuts, its’
seasonality, frequency, etc. Therefore the information about reliability of power supply is rather
subjective.

1.4.4. Below are the answers to the asked questions on reliability of power supply systems, received
from oral answers of RHCs staff, as well as in written from RHCs managers.

Clarification to Table 4.1.:

1. The data received as oral answers is highlighted in grey. The rest of the answers is received in

1.4.6 Conclusions:

1.4.6.1. The problems with periodic power cuts exist in all of the surveyed RHCs (see the raw 10).
The most unfavorable from this point of view period continues from November till March for the
RHCs located in Tashkent Region; for Pakhtakor RHC this period covers continues from September
till April, while for Juzim-Bog RHC this period covers continues from November till May (see the
raw 1).

1.4.6.2. Four out of five RHCs have no problems with the quality of the supplied power. The
exception is N.Byamuhammedov RHC, which faces this type of problems (see the raw 11).

1.4.6.3. In all of the RHCs power supply may be switched off during both day and night times (see
raw 8).

1.4.6.4. The most lengthy occasional power supply shut-down period was observed in A. Umarov
RHC – 7 days. In two other RHCs located in Tashkent Region (Jahonobod and N. Baymuhammedov)
such shut-downs had durations of 24 and 48 hours respectively (see the raw 1). In RHCs located in
Aral region such occasional shut-downs had not been observed.

1.4.6.5. In A. Umarov RHC the maximal length of “once a month shut-down” is 48 hours. In two
other RHCs (Pakhtakor and N. Baymuhammedov) periods of such shut-downs was 24 and 36 hours
respectively (see the raw 4).

1.4.6.6. In A. Umarov RHC the maximal duration of frequent shut-downs so-called “2-3 times a
week” was 24 hours. For the rest of the RHCs such frequent shut-downs had duration of 6-12 hours
(see the raw 6).

1.4.6.7. Minimal interval of 5 hours between two consecutive blackouts was observed in Jahonabod
RHC. Typical values for this period vary between 12-24 hours, while maximal values of 48-72 hours
in N. Baymuhammedov and Juzi Bog RHCs (see the raw 9).

1.4.6.8. The most unfavorable conditions during a month without power supply can be observed in A.
Umarov abd N. Baymuhammedov RHCs – the periods reach 20 days and 14 days respectively (taking
into account complaints of N. Baymuhammedov RHC on power quality). For the rest of the RHCs
these periods averaged 10±1.5 days taking into account night time blackouts and 2.5 - 5 days without
night-time blackouts.

109

UNDP- UZB -03 #00054196

Feasibility Study Report

Analyzing the data presented in Tables 5.1-5.10 the following conclusions on similarities and
dissimilarities of the areas where the surveyed RHCs are located from applicability of PV-systems as
standby power supply:

1. Maximal wind speed in all of the surveyed regions is practically the same and varies between 19-23
m/sec. The most unfavorable from installation of PV-systems point of view wind directions (south,
north, north-west) can be observed in the following three out of five surveyed RHCs: Jahonabad,
Pakhtakor, and Juzim Bog (see Tables 5.1.-5.4.).

2.The note on the duration of sunny periods in the area of Jahonabod RHC: the data of Chimgan
weather station (see the Table 5.6.) most likely does not adequately reflect the situation in Jahonabod
RHC. This seems to happened because of the readings of Chimgan weather station were taken in
unshaded area, while Jahonabod RHC is located in shaded area (see the Table 2.1.). Therefore factual
data on duration of sunny periods in this RHC is 20% less than shown in the Tables 5.6.

3. Irradiance by solar radiation (see the Tables 5.9 – 5.10) for all of the regions is almost the same for
all of the regions, and the variations does not exceed 10%. However these 10%, apparently represent
the values of systemic difference of the considered parameter – irradiance is the maximal in the areas
located close to Aral Sea.

It also should be mentioned that irradiance does not fully depends on season - the difference between
maximal and minimal values S does not exceed 10% of this indicator.

1.6. PROPOSALS ON OPTIMIZATION OF POWER CONSUMPTION AT RHC

1.6.1. Optimization of power consumption is one of the key issues for elaboration of FS for
establishment of standby system of power supply for RHC. This is mainly related to the fact that the
designed electric power consumption even for priority equipment and lighting in priority premises are
quite high.

Simple analysis of power inputs of RHC shows that in all surveyed RHCs there are ways and
approaches for their reduction. Review of these approaches and adequate recommendations for their
optimization and reduction – this is a direct way to reduce the cost of any standby system of power
supply.

1.6.2. There are two most simple and widespread approaches to reduce electric power consumption:

- 1-approach lies in reduction of equipment’s hours of service, reduction of general lighting system
and shift (at least partial) to local lighting system (table lamps and luminaries);

- 2-approach lies in replacement of existing equipment with increased power consumption on the
similar equipment, but with less power inputs.

1.6.2.1. Methods and ways for implementation of the first approach – this is most probably
prerogative of RHC employees and they can be covered in this report only in the form of most general
recommendations.

Methods and ways for implementation of the second approach in less extent depend on RHC
employees and mainly related to availability or lack of funds for procurement of new equipment.

To this end, we would only point out that all equipment (both medical and diagnostic, and auxiliary),
used in surveyed RHC, can be divided into two groups:

a/ 1-group – these are all physiotherapy and stomatological equipment, as well as some items of
auxiliary equipment (refrigerators of Soviet manufacture, old medical lighters, etc.), which had been
manufactured in nineties of the 20th century. Obviously, this equipment, used for more than 15 years,
could scarcely be considered as power-saving. Requirements to supply voltage is usual – 220V±10%

Amongst the equipment of this group, there is highly power-consuming equipment (see Table 3.1. and
3.2.), with high installed capacity (~ 500 W) and manufactured by rule-of-thumb methods (for
example, light bath at both Jakhonabod and Pakhtakor RHCs). Of course, the issue of appropriateness
of further operation of such equipment should be resolved by physicians themselves- only they can
impartially and comprehensively decide whether to operate this equipment or not.

b/ 2-group – these are diagnostic and auxiliary equipment received on National Programme Health-1.
Equipment of this group is manufactured and commissioned in the beginning of the 21st century (not

110

UNDP- UZB -03 #00054196

Feasibility Study Report

earlier than 2003) and, most probably belongs to the power-saving category. In fact, this group of
equipment could raise some question regarding practicability of RHC equipping with refrigerators of
large useful capacity, which causes high consumption (despite their efficiency) of electric power.
Requirements to supply voltage are also standard.

1.6.2.2. Situation with refrigerators in surveyed RHC is shown in Table 6.1.

It appears from Table that electric power consumed by refrigerators is rather high, especially at those
RHC, where three refrigerators are operated (see also Table 3.2. and 3.4.).

Share of electric power consumed only by refrigerators for drugs’ storage, in electric power consumed
by the most important auxiliary equipment, varies from 40 to 65% for various RHC (even after RHC
re-equipment with specialized refrigerators).

At the same time, there are both specialized (medical), and domestic refrigerators which consume two
times less electric power than refrigerators listed in Table 6.1. Including:

- specialized (medical) refrigerators of KYOCERA (presumably, US) designed for payload volume 39
l and at ambient temperature 32°С consume 280W*hour/day (without production of ice) and 470
W*hour/day with production of ice;

- domestic refrigerators, type Saratov 452KSh120 designed for payload 100 l and consume 630
W*hour/day.

It is easily seen that use of above specialized refrigerator would allow to reduce power inputs of
priority auxiliary equipment for 500 – 700 W*hour/day and on that basis either to reduce the capacity
of standby power supply (and, therefore, its cost), or additionally include to the list of priority
equipment, for example, cabinet for sterilizing oven.

Decision on implementation or non-implementation of above recommendations should be made by
physicians – specialists in the field of medical care technologies.

112

1.6.2.3. Efficient electric lighting system is one of the most important issues for elaboration of
efficient standby power supply.

This problem could be resolved by various approaches. However, the most radical one is to use so
called power-saving fluorescent lamps (PSL).

These lamps are characterized with two features:

- they possess all lighting technology characteristics of lamps normally applied for lighting (for
example, filament lamps, or tube fluorescent lamps), but consume substantively less (up to five times)
electric power,

- their nominal operating duration stated in specifications is 8000 – 10000 hour.

For comparison, filament lamps (FL) have a stated nominal operating duration of 1000 hour, and tube
fluorescent lamps (TFL) - 8000 – 10000 hour.

Naturally, an issue of application of these lamps should primarily be determined by reasons of
economy, since their current cost in Uzbekistan for 10 – 40 times higher the cost of filament lamps
and for 2 – 8 times higher the cost of TFL. Such a huge spreading of their cost relates to the fact that
in the Uzbekistan market PSL is sold by three groups of suppliers:

- Chinese PSL, the cheapest (1.8 – 2.5 $ depending on capacity), and at the same time most
unreliable. Their realistic average lifetime does not exceed 2 – 3 thousand hour. Moreover, in most
cases, their starting controllers do not comply with security requirements of IEC 928:1995, IDT,

- Local PSL (JSC FOTON) costs 4.0 – 4.5$, their average lifetime is 4.5 - 5 thousand hour, and they
fully comply with security standards of IEC 928:1995, IDT,

- European PSL (Philips, Osram, GE, and Siemens) in the Uzbekistan market costs 8 – 9$, their
average lifetime is 7 – 9 thousand hour, and they fully comply with security standards of IEC
928:1995, IDT.

To have a clear picture, note that cost of FL in the Uzbekistan’s market is 0.24 – 0.28$, and TFL – 1.0
– 1.2$.

1.6.2.4. All above data, plus information on current cost of one kW*hour electric power in
Uzbekistan, as well as average, minimum (Greece) and maximum (Denmark) costs of one kW*hour
of electric power in Europe, are summarized in Table 6.2.

2. ** - component cost indicated at lower level.

3. *** - actual hours of service differs from operating time stated in specifications because all
manufacturers declare continuous operating time. At the same time, all manufacturers world-wide
show in specifications that lamps resources might be decreased by more than two times in large
quantities of on-off cycles.

4. **** - number before “/” is taken from specifications, number after “/” – actual value.

Based on data described in Table 6.2, it would be possible quite to easily assess conditions, at which
the replacement of filament lamps and tube fluorescent lamps could become economically sound and
rational.

1.6.2.5. Operational costs for light (E) mainly comprise two parts – cost of lamps (Cl) and electric
power costs (Epc):

E = Cl + Epc

During the assessment period Т cost of lamps (Cl) would equal to:

Cl = Lp*(Т/tф+1), where

- Lp – lamps’ price in Column 2, Table 6.2.;

- tф – actual hours of service lamps in column 3 table 6.2.

During the period Т electric power costs (Epc) would equal to:

Epc = Poe*P*Т/1000, where

- Poe – price for one kW*hour electric power in Column 2, Table 6.2.

- P – capacity of lamps in Column 1, Table 6.2.

Apparently replacement of conventional lighting resources (filament lamps and tube fluorescent
lamps) with PSL would be economically sound and rational, should as a result of such replacement
the following conditions is observed:

Ecr ≥ Epsl , where

- Ecr – operational costs for light when conventional lighting resources are used;

- Epsl - operational costs for light when PSL is used.

1.6.2.6. Outcomes of calculations of Ecr, Epsl and E for different conditions are listed in Table 6.2,
and values Т=3000hour and tф fl= 300 hour, tф tfl = 3000 hour tф psl Uzbekistan= 3000 hour tф psl Eur= 5000
hour are listed in Table 6.3.

It should be noted that in Table 6.3, outcomes of calculations for Chinese PSL are not listed, since
they do not comply with the international security requirements (see column 4 Table 6.2.).

Table 6.3 Outcomes of calculations of operational costs for light

Name of object for calculations and conditions of calculations

2. TFL Р=2х40 W, at Poe =0.032 $US (Uzbekistan)
3. PSL (manufacture Uzbekistan) Р=20 W, at Poe =0.032 $US
(Uzbekistan)
4. PSL (manufacture Eur) Р=20 W, at Poe =0.032 $US (Uzbekistan)
5. Filament Lamp Р=100 W, at Poe =0.1 $US (min. Eur)

1.6.2.7. Outcomes of calculations quoted in Table 6.3 explicitly show that even in condition of low

illumination is replaced with wall illumination (with the help of wall lamp-brackets and wall sockets
that exist in each room – see Table 2.1.).

1.6.3.1. From Table 6.4 it is seen that the spreading of size of electric power shortage related to its
blackout is relatively high and comprises 17.5 kWh to 62 kWh on maximum value and 15.5 kWh to
49 kWh on average value.

At that, if extreme shortage of electric power is discarded apparently caused by accidents in power
transmission
lines (for example, one-time deficiency of electric power at Umarov and
Boimukhamedov RHC within 7 days and 4 days respectively), then the most typical value of electric
power shortage is ~ 40±5 kWh.

Indeed, this value seemingly should be considered as source information for selection of optimum
approach for arrangement of standby power supply in the surveyed RHC.

117

UNDP- UZB -03 #00054196

Feasibility Study Report

1.6.4. As it is seen from above, arrangement of standby power supply designed for monthly shortage
of electric power ~ 40 kWh would allow in surveyed RHC to carry out nearly all assignments in full
scope.

However, this value of shortage might be unacceptable owing to financial or any other reasons.

Based on that, we shall consider alternative variant that is less power-consuming. This variant is
based on proposals from the leadership of Umarov and Boimukhamedov RHCs. Essence of proposals
comprise of:

- only electrocardiograph and equipment for analysis should be referred as important medical and
diagnostic equipment;

- only refrigerator for drugs’ storage, medical lighters and equipment for disinfection of premises
should be referred as important auxiliary equipment;

- only laboratory, inspection, procedures and physiotherapy rooms should be referred as important
rooms.

Functioning of exactly these rooms defines ability of RHC to conduct, if not all but at any case, main
and constitutive functioning.

For the sake of objectivity, it should be noted that no one has ever offered such radical proposals so
far. However, we will review above variant and give a status of alternative variant.

in alternative variant, which has been mentioned in Clause 6.4 are shown in Table

1.6.6. At that, during these calculations the following were considered:

- power consumption (apart from equipment and lighting, which has been mentioned in Clause 6.4.)
lighting in entrance halls, corridors and stairs, as well as lighting in wards of hospital and labour ward
at Jakhonabod RHC,

- application of modern power-saving lamps for lighting,

- night consumption as described in Clause 2 Clarifications to Table 6.5.,

- equipping of Umarov and Boimukhamedov RHCs as per National Programme Health-2.

PART 2. OF THE REPORT OF THE NATIONAL SPECIALIST ON PV SYSTEMS:

2.1. DOWNTIME AND DEFICIT OF ELECTRIC POWER

2.1.1. The following assumptions were made when calculating RHC equipment downtime during the
most unfavorable autumn-winter period:

- duration of one-time daily equipment downtime (Тi) is equal to the period of time during workday
without electric power (from Table 4.1), while total period of monthly downtime Тм is equal to the
sum of periods of one-time downtimes during a month;

- duration of a workday (Тдр) assumed as 12 hours, while total number of work hours in a month –
under 6 workdays a week – is taken as 72 hours.

As for blackouts during spring-summer season, according to both written and oral data received from
RHCs management “there are blackouts during this period, though too rare”. Based on these
statements during calculations of monthly downtime it was assumed that during spring-summer
period total duration of monthly downtime is not more than 25% of downtime during autumn-winter
period.

The only exception was observed in Juzim Bog RHC, which reported about more intensive spring
blackouts compared with winter ones.

Table 1.1 presents data on actual downtime during autumn-winter time and average annual forecasted
downtime for five of the surveyed RHCs (without power cuts caused by breakdowns, which happened
once a season).

2.1.2. If the duration of blackout is equal to the duration of workday, and/or if equipment is meant to
be working uninterruptedly, then it is simple to identify the equipment downtime.

If this is not the case, then it is more complicated to identify the duration of downtime. For instance, if
the duration of downtime was 3 hours during a workday, then does this mean that the equipment
downtime would be also 3 hours? Apparently it is not always true as the equipment which is
operational only one hour a day can be used during the same day when blackout is finished. In this
case there will be no equipment downtime neither electric power deficit.

1.3. The problem of identification of downtime duration shall be a subject for discussion between
technicians and medical service providers.

Under such unclear conditions of identification, there are two options for calculation of power deficit:

The first option suggests to calculate total daily power consumption and power cuts during a month in
days accordingly. Product of one volume and the other one would give monthly deficit of electric
power caused by power cuts.

The second option requires differentiation of night time and day time power consumption during a
month, as well as calculation per hour power consumption separately for day and night times. The
sum of products (night time blackouts by per hour night time consumption and duration of day time
blackouts by per hour daily consumption) shall give monthly deficit of electric power caused by
power cuts.

As the way of calculation of power deficit is described in the 1st Report, this report presents power
deficit calculation in accordance with the second option:

2.2. PV-SYSTEM PARAMETERS CALCULATION

2.2.1. The methodology of calculation of PV-system parameters is based on the balance between
power consumption and amount of power which can be generated by PV-panel during some specified
period of time. This period is called base period and can be equal either a day, or week, or decade, or
month, etc. A calendar month is taken is a base period in this report.

The following data are considered to be input data for this process: energy received from PV-system
(NLo) and solar radiation parameter (S); output data would be generating capacity of the PV panel
(Ppv) and storage battery capacity (СBat), which is used as buffer between PV-panel and power
consumers.

The following shall be used to ensure accuracy of the calculations:

- maximum allowed depth of discharge of storage battery (Z). For modern batteries this figure should
not exceed 0.3, since under Z>0.3 their durability significantly reduced;

- losses in charge circuit of accumulators (or controller performance coefficient), as well as losses in a
cycle of accumulators charge-discharge (K Bat),

- temperature coefficient of accumulator capacity (KT Bat),

- losses in circuits of power consumers (KLo),

- voltage (DC) on system output, and invertor’s input (UDC),

- temperature coefficient of PV-panel power (KT PV),

- type of voltage-current characteristics of PV-panel.

2.2.2. This report used a model when daily electrical power demand varied during base period from 0
(when power was supplied) to maximal values (during day time power cut periods).

At the same time, the periods with maximal values of consumed energy from PV-system were
“synchronized” with the lowest solar radiation (i.e. periods of overcast sky). It is obvious, that such
approach in developing model for calculation parameters of PV-system determines the worst
conditions from power generation point of view, and imposes higher demands to PV-system and
accumulators. It also requires knowledge of actual changes of daily solar radiation values during base
period.

2.2.3. Within the framework of the model described in section 2.2., the generating capacity of PV-
panel (Ppv) and storage battery capacity (СBat ) were calculated by the following formulas:

Ppv = 1000*b* NLo .m/ a*Sm(1-KLo)*(1-KBat), where

Sm –total value od solar radiation for the base period (1 month),

NLo .m – total power capacity for the base period (1 month) consumed from PV-system ( Values for NLo
.m indicated in 7th row of Table 1.2.)

b = Uz/UDC, where Uz – voltage needed for charging accumulator (for the system with voltage of UDC
=12 V, Uz ≈ 15 V),

a = Pz/Ppv, where Pz – power generated by PV-panel under voltage of Uz. Usually, this value for the
most types of panels is equal to 0.9. This figure gets closer to 1 as Uz and UDC values increase–This is
one of the reasons for using so-called high-voltage PV-systems.

KBat,- (see 2.1) ≈ 0.2

KLo ,- (see 2.1) ≈ 0.15.

СBat = MAX{ KT Bat/[Z*(1-KLo)* UDC]}*[ ΣNLo .i – (NLo .m*ΣSi)/Sm), where

121

UNDP- UZB -03 #00054196

Feasibility Study Report

KT Bat – temperature coefficient of accumulator capacity. Value of KT Bat depends on the type of the
used accumulator battery (for the temperature of 20º С KT Bat ≈ 1.1),

NLo .i – daily power consumed from the system (precise values of NLo I are shown in the Table 1.2.)

Si – daily solar radiation

In the last formula the summation is made for all of the days of the base period (1 month).

2.2.4. Both of the formulas use the value of daily solar radiation S – solar energy received by the
surface of the PV-panel during daylight hours.

Under current report values of S were calculated on the basis of input data presented by Uzbek
Glavgidromet.

The following defections were used:

- “clear day– a day when sunshine duration (Тя) not less than 80% of duration of astronomical
daytime. (Та);

- “overcast day ”– a day when sunshine duration (Тп) not more than 20% of duration of astronomical
daytime (Та);

- “cloudy day with the average cloudy conditions”– a day, a day when sunshine duration (То) not less
than 20% and not more than 80% of duration of astronomical daytime (Та).

Based on these, the following assumptions over durations of sunshine were made:

- for clear day - Тя= 0.9*Та,

- for cloudy day with the average cloudy conditions - То = 0.5*Та,

- for overcast day -Тп=0.1*Та.

Shadowing with the duration of 25% of the astronomical daytime of the potential area where the PV-
system would be installed was taken into account for the area of Jahonobod RHC location.

When calculating solar radiation during cloudy days (with average cloudy conditions) a radiation
weakened by clouds also was taken into account. Sky radiation was not taken into account in
calculations.

Table 2.1. presents values of S for clear, cloudy and overcast days of December and January in the
areas of all of the surveyed RHCs. It also sets out data on number of clear, cloudy and overcast days,
as well as total values of solar radiation (Sm ) and average data for December and January. For area of
Juzim Bog RHC the above-mentioned data also presented for April.

Table 2.1. Solar Radiation and Cloudiness

2.2.7. Even without any charge of accumulators (for in case of abundant snowfall when PV-panel

2.3. PV-SYSTEM SPECIFICATIONS

2.3.1. This supplement shall be considered as general recommendation on PV-system kitting rather
than instructions to follow.

2.3.2. Selection of types of PV-systems, accumulators, controllers and invertors shall be proceeded
with the selection of working voltage of the PV-system ( UDC in the para 2.1. of the Report 2). From
energy point of view bigger values of UDC are more profitable as losses in both inverter and line
connecting PV-panel with the controller decrease with increasing of the voltage. Therefore, for the
designed PV-systems UDC voltage is selected at the level of 48 volt. This voltage is the most typical
for PV-systems with the installed power capacity between 1 and 3 kilowatt.

2.3.2.1. TSM type of modules manufactured by Telecom-STW (Russia) company is taken as base
type of PV-models. This type of modules has the following advantages:

- relatively low cost of one watt of installed capacity - $ 5.3 including transport and tax expenses.
Price of the selected type of modules is within the range of permitted price intervals – see the Table
3.1. of the report 2.

- availability of wide range of modules in terms of capacity and its’ modifications for the working
voltage of 24 volt. This makes adjustment of PV panel for working voltage of 48 volt more simple
and convenient.

- anti-vandal design of modules .

Main characteristics TSM type of PV-modules:

which can be provided by PV-panel.

shown in column 5 of the Table 1.1.

2.3.2.3.2. The main difficulty in selecting controller with the required parameters is linked with the
selected working voltage of 48 volt. Most of the controllers designed for the voltage of 12 or 24 volt.

There are two types of controllers available designed for working voltage of 48 volt:

a) C40 type of controller, produced by company XANTREX. It is designed for voltage range of 12-48
volt and for limiting current of 40 A. It can be seen form the Table 1.5 that this type of controller can
be used in PV-systems of all RHCs except Jahonobod RHC. C40 type of controller costs $195
without transportation and customs costs.

b) SPV-PWM-II/10A, SPV-PWM-II/20A, SPV-PWM-II/30A, SPV-PWM-II/40A и SPV-PWM-
II/50A types of controllers, designed for the work of PV-systems with the working voltage of 48 volt
with limiting current range of 10-50A. These controllers are being produced by SHENZHEN
WANFUJING (China) and can be used in all of the RHC.

Costs of controller for limiting current:

- 30А - $ 250,

- 40А - $ 290,

- 50А - $ 350.

The abovementioned prices include transportation and customs expenditures for procurement of one-

128

UNDP- UZB -03 #00054196

off products. Under bulk purchasing the prices can be reduced by 25 – 30%.

2.3.2.4. The following criteria used in selection of inverters:

- possibility to work under input voltage of 48 volt

- output power exceed total power of simultaneously working consumers

- possibility of short-time overload on output power (to ensure operation of refrigerators, TV-sets, and
other electric devices with high starting current)

2.3.2.4.2. Thus, for all of the RHCs inverter output power shall be not less than:

- 4,000 watt – for main option, and

- 2,000 watt – for alternative option.

Selection of inverters shall be made individually for every RHC. However it should be noted that
equipping of RHC with modern equipment shall be continued. Therefore National Specialist on PV-
systems believes that RHCs shall be equipped with inverters having margin of output power:

- 5,000 watt - for main option, and

- 3,000 watt - for alternative option

This would enable not only future development of RHC, but also improve operational reliability of
inverters.

2.3.2.4.3. As it was stated in sections 3.3. and 3.4. of the Report No. 2, one watt of output power cost
between $1.2-1.5.

MAP ENERGY Company (Russia) manufactures inverters, designed using principles of low-
frequency transformation, which significantly increases its’ reliability and overload characteristics
(see WWW SOLARHOME.RU). Its’ prices significantly lower: BBP-5 type of inverter with output
power of 5,000 watt cost as much as $1,700, while BBP-2 type of inverter with output power of 2,000
watt -$900 (including transportation and customs expenditures). Using of these inverters would be
very efficient.

Unfortunately, the list of the manufactured inverters has no inverters with output power of 3,000 watt,
and the issue of it’s procurement shall be considered separately.

2.4. MANUFACTURERS AND PRICES. MAINTENANCE SERVICES

2.4.1. PV-system market is not developed. Thus in 2006 the following types of PV-systems were
imported to Uzbekistan: Chinese with the price of $8-$11 per watt of generating capacity and Israel
made $10-$14 per watt of generating capacity. Recently Indian PV-systems appeared in the market
for the price of $16-$18 per watt. The abovementioned prices not included transport and customs
costs amounted as much as 15-20% of the initial prices.

2.4.2. According to the information of PV-systems National Specialist there is only one company in
Uzbekistan JSC FOTON producing PV systems with the following installation and providing after
sale services. The PV-systems produced by JSC FOTON cost $9-$10 per W of generating capacity
depending on specification.

2.4.3. At the end of this section we present data on market prices some of the components of PV-
systems published in RENEWABLE ENERGY Magazine, Vol. XV, No. 1 2001 – 2002:

2.6. RECOMMENDATIONS

2.6.1. National Specialist on PV systems has no available data on the costs of other options for
ensuring reliable power supply. It is obvious that proposed PV-system option is rather expensive.
However, is this the main factor to supersede the other factors?

2.6.2. Form O&M point of view PV system considered to be the most easiest system to carry out
maintenance work if the systems use sealed-in accumulators. Good example of almost 4-years
operation of domestic PV-systems can be seen in Kostruba village of Karakalpakstan, which is one of
the most isolated villages of Uzbekistan.

At the same time, all other systems, which could be used for ensuring reliable power supply, require
serious maintenance services. Particularly, using of UPS require maintenance of accumulators, as they
work mainly in buffer mode. Under such conditions using sealed-in accumulators is not
recommended due to higher corrosion behavior. Therefore it is difficult to introduce regular
maintenance of accumulators in Jahonobod RHC, which would include more frequent inspection and
changing structure of electrolyte.

4 out of 5 surveyed RHC have no professionally and regularly maintained electric systems. Only
Jahonobod RHC reportedly has qualified electrician.

2.6.3. Speaking about tariffs, introduction of UPSc with the aim of improving reliability of power
supply would result in increase of power consumption by 15-20% due to losses in accumulator circuit
charge. Apart from that, because of the low quality of energy in N. Baymuhamedov RHC the chargers
should be equipped with special voltage stabilizers, which would result in more losses of power.

Introduction of PV-systems allows the entire medical-diagnostic complex of RHC to switch to
autonomous power supply during spring-summer period due to higher solar radiation (not less than
three times compared with autumn-winter time), resulting in energy-conversion efficiency three times
higher during this period.

Surely, under existing low power tariffs such power losses would not be significant. However, if the
tariffs would reach average European level, then it would seriously impact financial situation of RHC.

2.6.4. There are several RHCs located in remote areas and disconnected from electric power grid.
These RHCs receive electric power by means of subfractional horsepower generators run by diesel,
which results in problems of diesel supply and polluting environment.

The problem of reliable and sustainable power supply shall be settled only through PV due to absence
of hydro resources in these districts.

2.6.5. Based on the above mentioned the National Specialist on PV systems suggests:

To establish 3 (three) pilot PV systems in three RHCs under proposed Project: in N.
Baymuhammedov, Jahonabod, and Juzim-Bog RHCs

All of these RHCs are located in three different locations with different character of power
consumption.

It is advisable to install a UPS-based system in two other RHCs (Umarov and Pakhtakor). Closeness
of these 2 RHCs to big cities would ease providing maintenance of UPSc.

The decision over generating capacity of the abovementioned three pilot PV-systems (main or
alternative option) shall be made depending on financial possibilities of the proposed Project.

133

UNDP- UZB -03 #00054196

Feasibility Study Report

PART 3 ANSWERS PROVIDED BY NATIONAL CONSULTANT ON PV SYSTEMS

TO ADDITIONAL QUESTIONS MADE BY INTERNATIONAL CONSULTANT

3.1. Question 1: To determine cost of installation for various RHCs in terms of economic and
technical approaches

3.1.1. Installation cost of PV system consists of two major parts:

• Part 1 is cost of metal constructions to be required to install PV panels at the site and cost of

accumulators’ placement;

• Part 2 is cost of PV system installation and adjustment at the site selected including costs of:

▪ materials and component parts required for the PV unit such as wires, cables, joints
and fittings, device for automatic switch on of emergency power supply source (in
case if this device will be included into design), etc.
labor cost for PV system installation.

▪

3.1.2. Cost of metal constructions (the first part) depends on installed capacity of the PV system, and,
therefore, a number of accumulators connected to the solar panel. Obviously, the large installed
capacity of PV system the greater cost is required for the installation works.

Moreover, this part of installation cost will be dependent on what option of the PV panel design
(ground or located at supports) the customer prefers (in our case the customer is the Ministry of
Healthcare): according to the data given in the RENEWABLE ENERGY Magazine, Vol. XV, No. 1,
2001 – 2002, ground located option in 1.5-2 times cheaper than option with supports at the same
number of PV modules.

Costs of PV system installation and adjustment (the second part) slightly depend on its installed
capacity. This part of installation cost is strongly depends on the object suitability for integration into
the emergency power supply system, its sizing, number of rooms which should be connected to
emergency power supply source, and other parameters that shall be indicated in the Terms of
Reference for placing the PV system at the concrete site.

3.1.3. Based on the above mentioned, it is evident that the real cost of PV installation can be
determined after receiving the precise terms of reference prepared by the customer (the Ministry of
Healthcare), development of detailed technical design for each of RHCs, and calculation of the cost
breakdown only. Therefore, the detailed development of this matter was not possible in the Report 4.
The possible installation cost for the case if the FOTON Plant will implement the installation has been
indicated indirectly in section 3.2 of the above Report. In this section, it has been said (quoted by the
Report 4) that ‘ cost of PV systems produced by the JSC FOTON that includes installation cost and
cost of after sale service during 3 years is 9-10 USD per W of installed capacity depending on the
providers of the PV system components’.

3.1.4. Without the concrete terms of reference and developed technical design, the very tentative
installation cost can be identified only. It is limited by the national consultant’s experience and
information available about the similar projects.

Based on the above said, data on the costs of

▪ metal construction required to install the PV panels is based on information given in the

RENEWABLE ENERGY Magazine, Vol. XV, No. 1, 2001 – 2002;

▪ metal construction required to place the accumulators and their installation and assembling is

based on the findings made during inquiries of the relevant expert of the JSC FOTON.

3.1.5. Cost of metal construction required to install the PV panels has been determined based on the
assumption that they will be installed at the metal supports above the ground (see section 1.2) and it is
given in Table 1.1. Such a solution was made as it is envisaged a safety (anti-vandal) location of the
PV panels. It has to be again emphasized that each company, which will make installation,
assembling and adjusting the PV system, has to choose together with the customer the optimal
solution to place the PV panels.

3.1.6. Results of the expert’s assessment of the cost of metal construction (stands) to place the
accumulators, and also the second part of cost for PV system installation (installation, assembling,

134

UNDP- UZB -03 #00054196

Feasibility Study Report

and adjustment) got from the queries of the specialist of FOTON Plant are given in Table 1.2.

The assessment made has had into account the follows:

▪
▪
▪

real cost of metal at domestic markets in Uzbekistan (as of 01.07.07);
tentative labor cost required for manufacturing of the stands;
shape of the power supply system available in each of the RHCs surveyed, RHC sizing, and the
required scope of works (see Table 2.1 of Report 3).
